Interpreting the Divine Attribute of Joy and Wonder
Razi interprets divine joy and wonder via ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses ta'wil to interpret anthropomorphic attributes like joy and wonder, affirming them while preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Divine joy and wonder are real attributes interpreted as pleasure and magnification, not emotions.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses two scriptural attributes: joy (al-farah) and wonder (al-ta'ajjub). Through careful ta'wil, he shows that these terms refer to God's pleasure and his magnifying of actions, not emotional changes. This lesson will guide you through his argument, revealing a consistent method for understanding anthropomorphic expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the two hadith about joy and cheerfulness. Notice the language of intense human emotion. Ask: how can we attribute such feelings to Allah?
The hadith use vivid imagery to emphasize the magnitude of divine joy.
2
Study Razi's ta'wil for joy
Razi says joy = pleasure (rida). Understand the principle: whoever is pleased with something rejoices. So for Allah, joy is his pleasure.
This shows that ta'wil connects the attribute to a compatible divine meaning.
3
Extend to wonder
Razi applies the same method: wonder = deeming something great. For Allah, it means he magnifies an act by reward or punishment.
The Quranic verse 'But I wonder' supports this interpretation.

Chapter Twenty-Eight: On Joy. From al-Nu'man ibn Bashir may Allah be pleased with him from the Prophet that he said: Allah rejoices more at the repentance of His servant than a man whose riding camel is lost in a barren land on a scorching day, and his riding camel carries his provision and water-skin. When his riding camel is lost, he is certain of destruction; and when he finds it, he rejoices at that. So Allah rejoices more at the repentance of His servant than this man. And he said: 'A man does not tread the mosques for prayer and remembrance except that Allah Almighty cheerfully welcomes him, more intensely than the family of an absent one cheer when their absent one returns to them.' The interpretation (al-ta'wil) is that whoever is pleased with a thing rejoices in it, so pleasure (al-rida) is called joy (al-farah). That is the meaning of cheerfulness. And from this category is his saying: 'Your Lord wonders at a young man who has no youthful passion.' And in another hadith: 'Your Lord wonders at three: people who line up in prayer, people who pray while fighting the polytheists, and a man who gets up to pray in the middle of the night.' And he recited: 'But I wonder and they mock' with damma on the ta'. That indicates the affirmation of this meaning with respect to Allah Almighty. Know that interpretation (al-ta'wil) is that wonder is a state that occurs when a matter is deemed great. So when Allah Almighty magnifies an act, either by its abundant reward or by its abundant punishment, it is permissible to apply the term wonder to Him. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an interpretive (ta'wil) argument about the divine attribute of joy (al-farah). Razi begins by quoting a hadith in which the Prophet describes Allah's joy at a servant's repentance exceeding a man's joy at finding his lost camel. He also cites a hadith about divine cheerfulness (bashasha) toward those who go to mosques. Razi then provides the interpretation: joy in God is not an emotional change but rather denotes divine pleasure (rida). He extends this to the attribute of wonder (ta'ajjub), as in the hadith 'Your Lord wonders at a young man...' and the Quranic verse 'بل عجبت' (But I wonder). Razi explains that wonder is a state of deeming something great; for God, it is permissible because it refers to God's magnifying an act by great reward or punishment. Thus, the passage establishes that such attributes are interpreted relative to God's perfection, focusing on the end-purpose (pleasure, magnification) rather than any physical or emotional change.

Interpreting Divine Modesty: The Universal Rule
Razi defines human modesty and proposes a universal interpretive principle for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's method for interpreting bodily attributes like modesty when applied to God, specifically the principle of focusing on ultimate purposes rather than initial physical states.

Core Idea
Divine modesty does not mean shame; it means generosity.

Razi begins by defining human modesty as a state of change and brokenness due to fear of blame. When this attribute is applied to God, it must be reinterpreted. He lays down a universal rule: bodily attributes are carried over to their ultimate purposes, not their physical beginnings. This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the Quranic verse and hadith that attribute modesty to God.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand Razi's definition of human modesty
Razi defines modesty as a change and brokenness overtaking a person due to fear of blame. He links it linguistically to 'life' (hayat).
This definition is crucial for seeing why ta'wil is needed.
3
Grasp the universal rule: nihayat al-aghard over bidayat al-a'rad
Razi states that attributes pertaining to bodies, when applied to God, are interpreted by their ultimate purposes, not their initial states. This is the key principle.
Remember: purpose not physical beginning.
4
Apply the rule to modesty
Razi gives the example: the beginning of modesty is physical change (taghayyur), but its end-purpose is avoiding blame or showing respect. So for God, modesty means He avoids blame by being generous.
This explains why God's modesty is about generosity, not shame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع والعشرون الحياء قال الله تعالى :: إن الله لا يستحى أن يضرب مثلا ما ، وروى سلمان - رضى الله عنه - عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن الله حى كريم، يستحى إذا رفع العبد يديه إليه ، أن يردهما صفرا . حتى يضع فيهما خيرا و اعلم : أن الحياء : تغير وانكسار يعترى الإنسان في خوف ما يعاتب ويذم به . واشتقاقه من الحياة . يقال : حي الرجل . كما يقال : نسى الرجل و خشی ، وسطى القوس, إذا أغفلت هذه الأعضاء . جعل الحياء ما يعتريه من الانكسار . والتغير متنكس القوة ، منتقص الحياة . ولهذا يقال : فلان هلك حياء من كذا ومات جياء من كذا ورأيت الهلال في وجهه من شدة الحياء ، وذاب حياء . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا بد من تأويله . وفيه وجهان : الأول : وهو أن القانون الكلى في أمثال هذه الصفات : أن كل صفة تثبت للعبد مما يختص بالأجسام . إذا وصف الله تعالى بذلك ، فهو محمول على نهايات الأغراض، لا على بدايات الأعراض، مثاله : أن الحياء حالة تحصل للإنسان، ولها مبدأ ونهاية . أما البداية فيها فهو التغير
Chapter Twenty-Nine: Modesty (al-Haya'). Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, Allah is not shy to strike a parable of a mosquito...' And Salman may Allah be pleased with him narrated from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, Allah is alive, generous; He is shy that when a servant raises his hands to Him, to send them back empty until He places good in them.' And know that modesty (al-haya') is a change and brokenness that overtakes a person in fear of what he may be blamed and reproached for. Its derivation is from life (al-hayat). It is said: 'The man lived' (hayya), just as it is said: 'The man forgot' (nasiya) and 'feared' (khashiya), and 'the bow became stiff' (satiya) when these organs are neglected. Modesty is made what overtakes one of brokenness and change, decreasing strength, diminishing life. Hence it is said: 'So-and-so died of modesty from such-and-such' and 'died of modesty from such-and-such' and 'I saw the crescent in his face from the intensity of modesty' and 'he melted from modesty.' When this is established, we say: its interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary. And regarding it there are two aspects. The first is that the universal rule concerning such attributes is that every attribute that is affirmed for the servant which pertains to bodies, when Allah Almighty is described by it, is applied to the ultimate purposes (nihayat al-aghard), not to the initial states (bidayat al-a'rad). Its example is that modesty is a state that occurs for a human; it has a beginning and an end. As for its beginning, it is the change...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is both a definition and an interpretive (ta'wil) argument about the divine attribute of modesty (al-haya'). Razi begins by quoting a Quranic verse and a hadith that attribute modesty to God. He then defines modesty in human terms: it is a state of change (taghayyur) and brokenness (inkisar) that occurs in a person due to fear of blame. He traces its linguistic derivation from 'life' (hayat). After establishing the human meaning, he asserts that such an attribute must be interpreted for God. He presents the first of two interpretative principles: the universal rule is that any bodily attribute affirmed for a human, when applied to God, is carried over to its ultimate purposes (nihayat al-aghard) rather than its initial physical states (bidayat al-a'rad). The example is modesty: its beginning is the physical change (taghayyur), but its end-purpose is avoiding blame or showing respect. Hence for God, modesty is interpreted as that which leads to generosity (like not sending hands back empty), not as a change. The passage ends mid-sentence but the core principle is clearly stated.

Misconception: One might think modesty in God implies a literal state of shame or weakness.
Correction: Razi clarifies that modesty is interpreted by its ultimate purpose (generosity), not by the physical change (taghayyur) that occurs in humans.

Razi's key principle is that attributes implying bodily change in humans are interpreted for God by focusing on their ultimate purpose, not their initial state.

The Meaning of Haya' (Modesty) in Language and Human Experience
Razi defines haya' linguistically and in human experience before applying it to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defines haya' (modesty) linguistically and in human experience, and why this human definition cannot be directly applied to God.

Core Idea
In human beings, haya' is a change and breaking; but in God, it must mean something entirely different.

Before we can understand what it means to say that Allah is 'modest' (Hayy), we must first understand the word haya' as it applies to humans. Imam Razi provides a detailed linguistic and experiential definition, tracing the word to its root in 'life'. This lesson unpacks that definition and the idiomatic expressions that surround it, setting the stage for a deeper theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence from Quran and Hadith
Begin by reading the verse and hadith that establish the attribute of haya' for Allah. Notice how they affirm modesty for God without explanation.
The hadith describes God's haya' in relational terms with supplicants.
2
Study Razi's human definition
Read the definition: 'تغير وانكسار' (change and breaking) that overcomes a person due to fear of blame. Ponder how this is a psychological state specific to humans.
3
Examine the etymology and idioms
Learn that haya' derives from 'life' (الحياة). Then look at the idiomatic expressions like 'perished from haya'' to see how deeply ingrained this concept is in Arabic.
This linguistic connection is crucial for later kalām arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع والعشرون: الحياء. قال الله تعالى: إن الله لا يستحي أن يضرب مثلاً. وروى سلمان رضي الله عنه عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: إن الله حي كريم يستحي إذا رفع العبد يديه إليه أن يردهما صفراً حتى يضع فيهما خيراً. واعلم: أن الحياء: تغير وانكسار يعتري الإنسان في خوف ما يعاتب ويذم به. واشتقاقه من الحياة. يقال: حي الرجل. كما يقال: نسى الرجل وخشي وسطى القوس إذا أغفلت هذه الأعضاء. جعل الحياء ما يعتريه من الانكسار والتغير متنكس القوة منتقص الحياة. ولهذا يقال: فلان هلك حياء من كذا ومات حياء من كذا ورأيت الهلال في وجهه من شدة الحياء وذاب حياء.
Chapter Twenty-nine: Al-Haya' (Modesty). Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, Allah is not ashamed to strike an example.' And Salman, may Allah be pleased with him, narrated from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: 'Indeed Allah is Hayy (Modest), Generous; He is modest, when a servant raises his hands to Him, to return them empty until He places good in them.' And know: that al-haya' is a change and breaking that overcomes a person in fear of what he may be blamed and censured for. Its derivation is from al-hayah (life). It is said: hayya al-rajul (the man was modest), just as it is said: nasiya al-rajul (the man forgot), khashiya (feared), and sata al-qaws (the bow was bent) when these organs are neglected. Haya' is made [to be] that which overcomes him of breaking and change, diminishing strength, reducing life. For this reason it is said: So-and-so perished from haya' of such-and-such, and died from haya' of such-and-such, and I saw the crescent in his face from the intensity of haya', and he melted from haya'.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage opens with the chapter heading 'Al-Haya'' and immediately cites scriptural evidence: the Quranic verse 'Indeed, Allah is not ashamed to strike an example' and a hadith describing Allah as modest ('Hayy, Karim') who is reluctant to return empty the hands of a supplicant. Razi then provides a definition of haya' as it occurs in human beings: a state of change and inner breaking that overtakes a person due to fear of blame or censure. He traces the etymology to 'life' (al-hayah), noting that the verb 'hayya' is used analogously to verbs like 'nasiya' (to forget) and 'khashiya' (to fear). The resulting state of haya' is described as diminishing one's strength and life-force—hence expressions like 'he perished from haya'' or 'he melted from haya''. This detailed human definition sets the stage for the need to interpret such an attribute when applied to God, since change and breaking cannot be attributed to the Creator.

Correction: Razi's definition shows that human haya' involves change and weakness, which cannot be attributed to God. Therefore, the divine attribute must be understood in a way that befits His perfection, often interpreted as the attribute of not leaving a supplicant empty-handed or as a form of divine generosity.

Razi's Hermeneutical Principle for Divine Attributes
Razi establishes a hermeneutical rule for interpreting bodily-based divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's universal principle for interpreting anthropomorphic attributes of God: that they refer to ends (outcomes) rather than beginnings (bodily changes).

Core Idea
Divine attributes refer to outcomes, not emotional processes.

Razi begins by asserting that certain attributes found in revelation must be interpreted allegorically. He lays down a universal rule: when an attribute involves a bodily or emotional process in humans, for God it only indicates the final result, not the initial change. This protects divine transcendence while preserving the scriptural text. In this lesson, we will unpack this principle and see how it works with two examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the necessity of interpretation
Razi opens by stating that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary for such attributes. Recognize that this is a response to literalism that would attribute change to God.
The phrase 'إذا ثبت هذا' refers back to a prior argument about divine transcendence.
2
Learn the universal principle
Identify the core principle: 'every attribute proven for the servant which is specific to bodies, if Allah is described by it, it is carried upon the ends of purposes, not upon the beginnings of accidents.' This is the hermeneutical key.
'Nihayat al-aghrad' (ends of purposes) and 'bidayat al-a'rad' (beginnings of accidents) are technical terms.
3
Examine the example of haya'
Razi analyzes haya' (modesty/shyness). In humans, it begins with a bodily change (blushing) due to fear of blame, and ends with refraining from the blameworthy action. For God, only the end (refraining) is intended.
This example shows how the principle is applied to a specific attribute.
4
Examine the example of ghadab
Similarly, ghadab (anger) in humans begins with boiling blood and desire for revenge, and ends with delivering punishment. When attributed to God, only the punishment is meant, not the emotional upheaval.
This reinforces the principle and shows its consistency.
5
Reflect on the purpose of the principle
Consider why Razi formulates this rule. It safeguards divine transcendence by denying that God undergoes change or passion, while affirming that the scriptural terms are meaningful in terms of their outcomes.
This principle is a common tool in Sunni theology for interpreting mutashabihat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لا بد من تأويله. وفيه وجهان: الأول: وهو أن القانون الكلي في أمثال هذه الصفات: أن كل صفة تثبت للعبد مما يختص بالأجسام. إذا وصف الله تعالى بذلك، فهو محمول على نهايات الأغراض، لا على بدايات الأعراض. مثاله: أن الحياء حالة تحصل للإنسان، ولها مبدأ ونهاية. أما البداية فيها فهو التغير الجسماني الذي يلحق الإنسان من خوف أن ينسب إلى القبيح. وأما النهاية فهي أن يترك الإنسان ذلك الفعل. فإذا ورد الحياء في حق الله تعالى، فليس المراد منه: ذلك اللحوق الذي هو مبدأ الحياء وتقدمته. بل المراد: هو ترك الفعل الذي هو منتهاه وغايته. وكذلك الغضب له مبدأ وهو غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام، وله غاية وهو إيصال العقاب إلى المغضوب عليه. فإذا وصفنا الله تعالى بالغضب، فليس المراد ذلك المبدأ - أعني غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام - بل المراد: تلك النهاية، وهي إنزال العقاب. فهذا هو القانون.
Once this is established, we say: it must be interpreted. And there are two ways: the first: which is the universal principle concerning such attributes: that every attribute proven for the servant which is specific to bodies, if Allah Almighty is described by it, it is carried upon the ends of purposes, not upon the beginnings of accidents. Its example: indeed haya' is a state that occurs for a human, and it has a beginning and an end. As for the beginning in it, it is the bodily change that overtakes a person from fear of being attributed to the ugly. And as for the end, it is that the person refrains from that action. So when haya' is reported regarding Allah Almighty, what is meant by it is not that occurrence which is the beginning and antecedent of haya'; rather, what is meant is the refraining from the action which is its outcome and purpose. And similarly anger has a beginning, which is the boiling of the blood of the heart and the desire for revenge, and it has an end, which is delivering punishment to the one against whom anger is directed. So when we describe Allah Almighty with anger, what is meant is not that beginning—I mean the boiling of the blood of the heart and the desire for revenge—but rather what is meant is that end, which is sending down punishment. This is the principle.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two interpretations for understanding attributes like haya' when applied to God. Razi begins by affirming that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary. He states a universal principle ('al-qanun al-kulli'): whenever an attribute is proven for a human servant that is specific to bodies (i.e., involves physical or emotional states), if that attribute is used for Allah, it must be understood as referring to the final purposes or outcomes (nihayat al-aghrad) rather than the initial bodily changes (bidayat al-a'rad). He illustrates with haya': in humans, haya' has a beginning—a bodily change due to fear of being blamed—and an end—the action of refraining from the blameworthy act. When attributed to God, only the end is meant: His refraining from returning empty-handed (as in the hadith). Similarly, anger in humans begins with boiling blood and desire for revenge, but ends with inflicting punishment. For God, only the end (punishment) is intended, not the emotional upheaval. This principle, Razi concludes, is the universal rule for such attributes.

Key Terms
تأويل
قانون كلي
نهايات الأغراض
بدايات الأعراض
الغضب
الحياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing emotions like anger or shyness to God implies literal emotional change in the divine essence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that only the external outcome (e.g., punishment) is intended, not the internal emotional process.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first interpretation, when haya' is attributed to God, what is intended?
A) The bodily change of blushing
B) The fear of being blamed
C) The end result of refraining from an action
D) The combination of beginning and end

Key Takeaway
Razi's universal principle safeguards divine transcendence by interpreting anthropomorphic attributes as referring to their ultimate outcomes, not their initial physical processes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about God's 'anger' (ghadab), do you instinctively imagine a human-like emotional reaction? How does Razi's principle expose that as a failure to properly magnify Allah? What concrete step can you take today to train your heart to think of Allah's attributes only in ways befitting His majesty?
?
Consider a moment you felt haya' (shyness) deeply. Now try to conceive of Allah's haya' as a real attribute without any bodily feelings or social fear. Does your mind struggle? Why? What does this struggle reveal about your need for deeper tawhid?
?
Razi says this principle is universal for all bodily attributes. Is there any attribute of Allah that you have been interpreting with human-like beginnings without realizing it? Will you commit to pausing whenever you encounter such attributes and consciously shifting your mind to the outcome only?

Interpreting God's Attributes: The Principle of Beginning and End
Razi explains the principle of interpreting attributes by distinguishing their beginning and end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret human-like attributes such as modesty and anger when applied to God, using the distinction between the beginning and the end of an attribute.

Core Idea
Divine attributes point to outcomes, not emotions.

When we read that God is 'angry' or 'modest', our minds may jump to human emotions. Razi clarifies that for God, only the end result of these attributes is meant—not the emotional or physical beginning. This lesson establishes the principle that underpins the interpretation of many divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses very precise language for beginning and end.
2
Identify the beginning and end for modesty and anger
Find the definitions of the beginning (mabda') and end (ghaya) for each attribute as described by Razi.
3
Understand why only the end is attributed to God
Ask yourself why Razi insists that only the end is meant for God. Consider what this says about divine transcendence.
This connects to the principle that God is free from physical and emotional changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسماني الذي يلحق الإنسان من خوف أن ينسب إلى القبيح، وأما النهاية فهي أن يترك الإنسان ذلك الفعل، فإذا ورد الحياء في حق الله تعالى، فليس المراد منه: ذلك اللحوق الذي هو مبدأ الحياء وتقدمته، بل المراد: هو ترك الفعل الذي هو منتهاه وغايته. وكذلك الغضب له مبدأ وهو غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام، وله غاية وهو إيصال العقاب إلى المغضوب عليه. فإذا وصفنا الله تعالى بالغضب، فليس المراد ذلك المبدأ – أعني غليان دم القلب وشهوة الانتقام – بل المراد: تلك النهاية، وهي إنزال العقاب. فهذا هو القانون.

والثاني: إن الذي لا يجوز على الله تعالى من جنس هذه الأوصاف. فهل يجوز ذكره على سبيل النفي عن الله تعالى؟ قال بعضهم: إنه لا يجوز إطلاق هذه الألفاظ على طريقة النفي، بل يجب أن يقال: إنه تعالى لا يوصف بها. فأما أن يقال: إنه لا يستحي ويطلق ذلك في حال، لأنه يوهم نفي ما يجوز عليه. وما ذكره الله تعالى في كتابه من قوله: لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم، ولم يلد ولم يولد، فهو وإن كان في صورة النفي، لكنه ليس في الحقيقة. بل المراد منه: نفي صحة الاتصاف. وكذا قوله: ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد، وقوله: ما اتخذ الله من ولد، وقوله: وهو يطعم ولا يطعم. وليس كل ما ورد في القرآن إطلاقه جاز أن يطلق في المخاطبات، بل الحق: أنه لا يجوز إطلاق ذلك إلا مع بيان أنه محال ممتنع في حق الله تعالى.

وقال آخرون: لا بأس بإطلاق هذا النفي، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية عن الله تعالى. فكان الإخبار عن عدمها صدقا، فوجب أن يجوز ذلك النفي. بقى أن يقال: إن الأخبار عن انتفائها يقتضى صحة إطلاقها عليه. إلا أنا نقول: هذه الدلالة ممنوعة. فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه. بل لو قرن باللفظ ما يدل على انتفاء الصحة أيضاً، كان ذلك أحسن من حيث إنه يكون مبالغة في البيان في إزالة الإيهام. وليس يلزم من كون غيره أحسن منه كونه في نفسه قبيحا. والله أعلم.
Body-based [shame] which befalls a person from fear that he would be attributed to something ugly. As for the end, it is that the person leaves that action. So when modesty (al-haya') is mentioned with respect to God Most High, what is meant by it is not that occurrence (al-luhuq) which is the beginning (mabda') of modesty and its precedence, but rather what is meant is the leaving of the action which is its end (muntaha) and its outcome (ghaya). Likewise, anger (al-ghadab) has a beginning, which is the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance, and it has an end, which is delivering punishment to the one upon whom anger is directed. So when we describe God Most High with anger, what is meant is not that beginning – namely the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance – but rather that end, which is the infliction of punishment. This then is the principle (al-qanun).

Second: That which is not permissible regarding God Most High among this kind of attributes. Is it permissible to mention it by way of negation (nafy) from God Most High? Some said: It is not permissible to utter these words by way of negation; rather, it must be said that He Most High is not described by them. So it is not said: 'He does not feel shame' and then to utter that in any state, because it may imply the negation of what is permissible regarding Him. And what God Most High mentioned in His Book, such as His saying: 'Slumber does not overtake Him nor sleep' (2:255), and 'He did not beget nor was He begotten' (112:3), although it is in the form of negation, it is not in reality [merely negation]; rather what is meant by it is the negation of the validity of attribution (nafy sihhat al-ittisaf). Likewise His saying: 'It did not befit God that He should take a child' (19:35), and His saying: 'God has not taken a child' (23:91), and His saying: 'And He feeds and is not fed' (6:14). And not everything that occurs in the Quran in its utterance is permissible to utter in discourses; rather the truth is that it is not permissible to utter that except with clarification that it is impossible and prohibited concerning God Most High.

And others said: There is no harm in uttering this negation, because these attributes are negated from God Most High. So the report about their absence is true, so that negation must be permissible. It remains to be said that the report about their absence implies the validity of uttering them concerning Him. But we say: This implication is prevented (mamnu'a). For the report about the absence of something does not indicate that that thing is permissible regarding Him or prohibited regarding Him. Rather, if one coupled with the wording something that also indicates the denial of validity, that would be better inasmuch as it would be an exaggeration in clarification for removing ambiguity. And it does not follow from the fact that something else is better than it, that it is in itself ugly. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a theological exposition of a principle (qanun) for interpreting certain attributes ascribed to God that originate in human experiences, and then a discussion on the permissibility of using negative statements about God regarding such attributes. 

The author, Imam Razi, begins by analyzing two attributes: modesty (haya') and anger (ghadab). He notes that each has a beginning (mabda') and an end (ghaya). For modesty, the beginning is the feeling of shame that deters one from an ugly act; the end is the act of leaving that act. For anger, the beginning is the boiling of the heart's blood and the desire for vengeance; the end is the actual infliction of punishment. When these attributes are applied to God, only the end—not the emotional or physical beginning—is intended. Thus, "God is angry" means He punishes, not that He experiences an emotional state. This is the established principle.

Razi then raises a second question: for attributes that are impossible for God (like human emotions), is it permissible to simply negate them—e.g., "God is not shy" or "God does not get angry"? He reports two positions. The first position holds that such naked negations are not allowed because they might wrongly imply that the attributes could be possible for God (i.e., that He could have them, even though He doesn't). Instead, one should say "God is not described by such attributes" to emphasize impossibility. Razi supports this by pointing to Quranic verses that appear as negations but actually negate the very validity of the attribution (nafy sihhat al-ittisaf), not just the occurrence. For example, "Slumber does not overtake Him" means it is impossible for God to be subject to slumber. He adds that not every Quranic expression can be used freely in ordinary speech; it is only permissible when accompanied by a clarification that the attribute is impossible for God.

The second position holds that simple negations are permissible because the attributes are truly absent from God, and truthful statements are allowed. Razi then critiques this position: reporting absence does not by itself indicate whether the attribute is possible or impossible for the subject. However, if one adds an indicator of invalidity, that is better for removing ambiguity. He concludes that while the better approach is to couple negation with a statement of impossibility, the mere negation is not necessarily ugly (qabih), though it is not the best. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Throughout, Razi demonstrates a nuanced method: he avoids both crude anthropomorphism and simplistic negation, insisting on careful linguistic and theological precision.

Key Terms
الحياء
الغضب
المبدأ
النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when God is described with anger, He experiences the same emotion as humans.
Correction: Razi clarifies that only the end result (punishment) is meant, not the emotional beginning.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's principle, when we attribute 'anger' to God, what is the correct understanding?
A) God experiences a boiling of blood
B) God desires vengeance
C) God inflicts punishment
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
When interpreting human-like attributes for God, we affirm only the outcome, not the emotional process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God 'gets angry' at sinners, do you subconsciously imagine a divine tantrum? How does this lesson force you to purge that image—and what does it replace it with? Will you let go of that comfortable but false picture today?
?
If God's anger is solely the infliction of just punishment, then His mercy must also be solely the bestowal of grace. How does that change your sense of accountability? Are you living as if the outcome of your deeds is what truly matters, or do you hide behind sentiment?
?
This principle strips away all human emotion from God's attributes. Is there any attribute of God you have secretly 'humanized'—thinking He 'feels' disappointment or pride? What would it cost you to surrender that image and worship a God whose actions are pure, untainted by passion? Are you ready to make that surrender now?

Negating Divine Attributes: The Permissibility of Negative Statements
Razi discusses whether it is permissible to negate impossible attributes from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether it is permissible to simply negate impossible attributes from God, and Razi's preferred position.

Core Idea
Negation of an attribute from God must avoid implying that the attribute could be possible.

After establishing how to interpret positive attributes, Razi tackles a new question: Can we simply say 'God is not shy' or 'God does not get angry'? He presents two scholarly views and their reasoning. This lesson unpacks that debate and Razi's nuanced conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two positions Razi reports.
2
Identify the two positions on negation
Find the first position that prohibits bare negation and the second that permits it. Note the reasoning and Quranic evidence cited.
3
Understand Razi's critical analysis and final ruling
Razi critiques the second position and gives his preferred approach. Pay attention to his conclusion that simpler negation is not ugly but not the best.
This connects to the principle of proper theological language.

Objection and Answer on Negating Attributes
Imam Razi responds to an objection about permissibility of negating divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against negating attributes of Allah and the author's response clarifying that reporting negation does not imply possibility.

Core Idea
Reporting absence does not imply possibility; negation can be truthful without attributability.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology about whether it is permissible to negate attributes of Allah. Some argued that since the negation is true, it must be permissible. An objector countered that negation implies the attribute could be attributed. Imam Razi responds with a precise logical distinction that resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the different voices: 'others said', 'it remains to be said', and 'we say'.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the claim that negation is permissible (first sentence) and the objection that reporting negation implies possibility (second sentence starting with 'بقى أن يقال').
The objection is introduced as 'it remains to be said'.
3
Analyze the author's answer
Look at the response starting with 'فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء...' The author denies the implication and suggests a stronger phrasing. Ask yourself: Why does the author think the objection fails?
The key phrase: 'لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال آخرون : لا بأس بإطلاق هذا النفي، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية الله تعالى . فكان الإخبار عن عدمها صدقا، فوجب أن يجوز ذلك. بقى أن يقال : إن الأخبار عن انتفائها يقتضي صحة إطلاقها عليه - إلا أنا نقول : هذه الدلالة ممنوعة . فإن الإخبار عن عدم الشيء لا دلالة فيه على أن ذلك الشيء جائز عليه أو ممتنع عنه بل لو قرن باللفظ ما يدل على انتفاء الصحة أيضاً، كان ذلك أحسن من حيث إنه يكون مبالغة في البيان في إزالة الإيهام . وليس يلزم من كون غيره أحسن منه، كونه في نفسه قبيحاً . والله أعلم
And others said: There is no harm in uttering this negation, because these attributes are negated of Allah Almighty. So reporting their non-existence is true, so it is necessary that it be permissible. It remains to be said: Reporting their negation implies the validity of attributing them to Him—but we say: This indication is not conceded. For reporting the non-existence of something contains no indication that that thing is possible for Him or impossible for Him. Rather, if one were to accompany the expression with something indicating also the negation of validity, that would be better insofar as it would be an exaggeration in clarifying the removal of ambiguity. And it does not follow from something else being better than it, that it is itself ugly. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion concerning the permissibility of negating attributes of Allah. The author presents the view of some who argue that negating attributes is permissible because the statement is truthful—since these attributes are indeed negated of Allah, reporting their absence is true, so it should be allowed. An objector then raises a point: that reporting the negation of these attributes implies that those attributes could conceivably be attributed to Allah (otherwise why negate them?). The author responds by denying this implication. He explains that reporting the non-existence of something does not indicate whether that thing is possible or impossible for the subject. Moreover, he adds that if one were to include a phrase that also negates the validity of applying the attribute, that would be even better as it removes ambiguity more forcefully. However, the fact that something else is better does not make the original statement itself ugly. The passage ends with 'And Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
نفي (negation)
صفات (attributes)
الإخبار عن عدم (reporting absence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Declaring negations about Allah implies those attributes are possible for Him.
Correction: Reporting absence does not indicate possibility or impossibility; it is simply a true statement.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, does reporting the non-existence of something indicate that it is possible for that thing to exist?
A) Yes, because negation presupposes possibility.
B) No, because reporting absence does not indicate possibility or impossibility.
C) Yes, but only if the attribute is a positive one.
D) No, unless the statement is explicitly conditional.

Key Takeaway
Negating attributes of Allah is permissible because a truthful statement about non-existence does not imply that the attribute could be attributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah, do you ever use negations (e.g., 'Allah is not unjust')? After reading this passage, are you aware of the potential implications of your words? How can you ensure your speech is both truthful and free from misleading implications? Commit to reviewing your own statements about Allah and adding clarifying phrases when needed.
?
This passage shows that even truthful statements can be improved to remove ambiguity. Is there an area of your understanding of Allah where you have settled for a correct but incomplete expression? What specific aspect of His attributes might you need to clarify further to avoid misunderstanding in your own mind or when teaching others?
?
The author emphasizes that what is better does not make the original ugly. Reflect on a time when you refrained from saying something true about Allah because you feared it might be misunderstood. Does this lesson give you the confidence to speak truthfully while also striving for greater clarity? Write down one commitment to speak more boldly about Allah's attributes, balanced with appropriate caution.

Second Hadith: The Book of Mercy Above the Throne
Reporting that Allah's mercy is recorded in a book above the Throne

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this hadith is interpreted by different theological schools and why it is significant in discussions of divine attributes.

Core Idea
The hadith about mercy and wrath is a key proof text for those who assert spatial location for Allah.

This hadith reports that Allah wrote a book stating His mercy prevails over His wrath, and the book is with Him above the Throne. While the literal wording seems to place the book—and by implication Allah—in a direction, mainstream Sunni theology interprets this figuratively. Let's explore the text and its doctrinal implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the phrasing 'فهو عنده فوق العرش' (it is with Him above the Throne).
The hadith is narrated by the author of Sharh al-Sunna from Abu Hurayra.
2
Identify the key controversial phrase
Focus on 'عنده فوق العرش' – 'with Him above the Throne'. This phrase implies a spatial relationship between the book and Allah.
Proponents of anthropomorphism use this to argue Allah has a location.
3
Understand the Ash'ari interpretation
Ash'ari scholars interpret 'with Him' metaphorically as 'in His knowledge' or 'under His decree', avoiding any spatial attribution to Allah.
They emphasize tanzih (transcendence) and reject direction for Allah.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider why this hadith is a major point of contention in kalam: it touches on the attributes of mercy and wrath and the issue of Allah's relationship to creation.
The phrase 'رحمتي سبقت غضبي' confirms mercy predominates, which is a key point for the Ash'ari view of divine grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر الثاني: ما روى صاحب شرح السنة في باب رسمة رحمة الله تعالى عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: لما قضى الله الخلق كتب كتابا، فهو عنده فوق العرش: إن رحمتي سبقت غضبي.
The second report: What the author of Sharh al-Sunna narrated in the chapter on the drawing of Allah's mercy, from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him: When Allah created creation, He wrote a book, and it is with Him above the Throne: 'My mercy prevails over My wrath.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This brief hadith is presented as further scriptural evidence. It states that when Allah created the heavens and earth, He wrote a book stating that His mercy overcomes His wrath. The book is described as being 'with Him above the Throne' (فهو عنده فوق العرش). The phrase 'with Him' using 'inda and 'above the Throne' explicitly place the book in a spatial relation to Allah, implying that Allah Himself is in a direction, or at least that what is with Him is located above. The proponents use this to argue for direction.

Key Terms
العرش
الرحمة
الغضب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith proves that Allah is physically located above the Throne.
Correction: The Ash'ari interpretation holds that 'with Him' means in His knowledge and decree, not spatial location. Allah is beyond time and place.

Checkpoint
Where is the book of mercy written by Allah located according to this hadith?
A) In the Preserved Tablet.
B) In the hearts of the believers.
C) With Allah above the Throne.
D) In the Garden of Eden.

Key Takeaway
The hadith's literal wording suggests spatial location for Allah, but it is understood figuratively in orthodox Islamic theology to preserve divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Allah's mercy 'prevails over His wrath.' Does your daily life reflect a deep trust in that mercy, or do you live more often in fear of His displeasure? What would change in your actions and thoughts if you truly let this truth sink into your heart?
?
The hadith teaches that mercy has already been placed in a book above the Throne. Do you live as though mercy is the final word for you, or do you behave as if your own misdeeds can outweigh His mercy? What concrete step can you take this week to remind yourself that mercy is greater?
?
If the book of mercy is 'with Him above the Throne,' how does that affect your understanding of where hope comes from? Are you seeking mercy from anything or anyone other than Allah, or are you relying completely on the mercy He has already decreed? What hidden idol of self-reliance might you need to break?

Four Objections to Istiwa as Istila and Their Answer
Razi presents four objections to interpreting istiwa as istila and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised against interpreting God's istiwa as istila and how the response redefines the term while also explaining the special mention of the Throne.

Core Idea
Objections to theological terms often stem from their literal meanings; answers refine definitions.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a classic objection-response exchange. The objector argues that interpreting istiwa as istila (domination) is impossible because the word implies weakness, opponents, or pre-existence of the object. Razi's answer first redefines istila to avoid these implications, then gives two reasons why the Throne is specifically mentioned. This unit shows how kalam thinkers defend metaphorical interpretations of Qur'anic terms by carefully redefining key concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Start by reading the four objections carefully. Each one points to a linguistic or theological problem with taking istiwa as istila.
Notice that each objection assumes a certain meaning of the word.
2
Understand the answer
Read Razi's response. He first redefines istila as 'perfect power free from opponent' to neutralize the objections. Then he addresses why the Throne is specified.
See how the redefinition directly counters each objection without changing the word.
3
Analyze the specification of the Throne
Examine the two reasons for specifying the Throne: 1) it is the greatest creation, 2) it serves as an intermediary for divine management (per al-Ghazali).
The second reason is based on a cosmological model that Razi may later critique.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how this exchange illustrates the kalam method: objections are dismantled by clarifying meanings, not by denying the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا التأويل غير جائز . لوجوه : الأول : إن الاستيلاء عبارة عن صول الغلبة بعد العجز ، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال . الثاني : إنه إنما يقال : فلان استوى على كذا ، إذا كان له منازع ينازعه وذلك فى حق الله تعالى محال . الثالث : إنه إنما يقال فلان استولى على كذا : إذا كان المستولى عليه موجودا قبل ذلك وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . لأن العرش إنما حدث بتكوينه وتخليقه . الرابع : إن الاستيلاء بهذا المعنى حاصل بالنسبة إلى كل المخلوقات. فلا يبقى لتخصيص العرش بالذكر فائدة . والجواب: إن مرادنا بالاستيلاء القدرة التامة الخالية عن المنازع والمعارض والمدافع . وعلى هذا التقدير، فقد زالت هذه المطاعن بأسرها . وأما تخصيص العرش بالذكر . ففيه وجهان : الأول : إنه أعظم المخلوقات ، فخص بالذكر لهذا السبب ، كما أنه خصه بالذكر في قوله : ه هو رب العرش العظيم لهذا المعنى . قال الشيخ الغزالي - رحمه الله . في كتاب إلجام العوام» : «السبب في هذا التخصيص : هو أنه تعالى يتصرف في جميع العالم ، ويدير الأمر من السماء إلى الأرض ، بواسطة العرش فإنه تعالى لا يحدث صورة في العالم مالم يحدثها في العرش كما لا يحدث النقاش والكاتب صورة البناء على البياض ، مالم يحدثها في الدماغ ، بل لا يحدث صورة البناء فى الخارج . مالم يحدث صورته في الدماغ ، بواسطة القلب والدماغ يدبر الروح أمر عالمه الذي هو يدبر فكذا بواسطة العرش يدبر الله أمر كل العالم
If it is said: this interpretation is not permissible. For several reasons: First: istila [taking over] is an expression of overcoming after inability, and that is impossible with respect to God Almighty. Second: it is only said 'so-and-so established [istawa] over something' if he has an opponent who contends with him, and that is impossible with respect to God Almighty. Third: it is only said 'so-and-so took over [istawla] something' if that over which he took over existed before that, and this is impossible with respect to God Almighty, because the Throne only came into being by His bringing into being and creation. Fourth: taking over in this sense is obtained with respect to all creatures, so there remains no benefit in specifying the Throne by mention. The answer: what we mean by istila is perfect power free from any opponent, adversary, or defender. On this estimation, all these objections are removed entirely. As for specifying the Throne by mention, there are two aspects: first, it is the greatest of creatures, so it is specified by mention for this reason, just as He specified it by mention in His saying 'He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' for this meaning. Shaykh al-Ghazali, may God have mercy on him, said in his book Iljam al-'Awam: 'The reason for this specification is that He, exalted, disposes in the entire world and directs the command from heaven to earth through the medium of the Throne. For He does not bring about any form in the world without first bringing it about in the Throne, just as the painter and writer do not bring about the form of a building on the blank page without first bringing it about in the brain, rather they do not bring about the form of the building externally without first bringing about its form in the brain through the medium of the heart. And the brain manages the command of its world which it manages; likewise through the medium of the Throne God manages the command of the entire world.'
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector raises four linguistic and theological arguments against interpreting the istiwa (establishment) of God over the Throne as istila (domination). First, istila implies overcoming after weakness, which is impossible for God. Second, istiwa (in the sense of settling) presupposes an opponent, impossible for God. Third, istila requires the dominated object to pre-exist, but the Throne is created by God, so it didn't pre-exist His act. Fourth, if istila is taken generally, then the Throne is not special, nullifying the specification. The answer redefines istila as 'perfect power free from any opponent'—this negates the first three objections because the term no longer implies weakness or opponent. Then it addresses the specification of the Throne: it is specified because it is the greatest creation (analogous to the verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne'), and also because, as al-Ghazali explains, the Throne is the intermediary through which God administers the world, similar to the role of the brain in human action. This explanation, however, is based on a particular philosophical cosmology, which Razi will later critique.

Key Terms
استيلاء
استواء
تأويل
عرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that interpreting istiwa as istila is invalid because istila always implies prior weakness or an opponent.
Correction: The author shows that istila can be redefined as 'perfect power free from opponent,' which removes those implications. The term's validity depends on the technical definition, not the literal sense.

Checkpoint
What is the main strategy the author uses to answer the first three objections?
A) He denies that istila means taking over.
B) He redefines istila as perfect power without prior weakness or opponent.
C) He argues that God can have attributes of weakness.
D) He says the objections only apply to created beings.

Key Takeaway
Theological terms must be understood by their intended definitions, not their literal senses, allowing for valid interpretations of ambiguous Qur'anic expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone objects to a belief you hold, do you defensively reject their objection, or do you carefully consider their reasoning as Razi does here? What might that say about your trust in the truth of your belief?
?
Razi refines the definition of 'istila' to answer objections. Has there been a time when you misunderstood a religious term and acted on that misunderstanding? Are you willing to revisit that term now with a more careful definition?
?
Al-Ghazali's explanation of the Throne as an intermediary for divine administration is profound. How does it change the way you think about God's relationship to the universe? Does it make His governance more intimate or more distant in your heart?

The Two Meanings of 'Above' (Fawq) in the Quran
Razi demonstrates the dual meaning of 'fawq' in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic word 'fawq' has two distinct meanings in the Quran: superiority in rank/power and spatial height, and you will see Quranic examples of both.

Core Idea
'Fawq' in the Quran often means power and rank, not physical direction.

Before we can judge whether verses about God being 'above' imply direction, we must first examine the range of meaning of the word itself. Imam Razi points out that the same word is used in the Quran for both spatial height and for superiority in power or rank. This lesson introduces that linguistic fact.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Notice how Razi begins by stating that the word 'fawq' is used in two ways: for rank/power and for direction.
The passage includes several Quranic verses as examples.
2
Identify the two meanings
Look for the two meanings: first, 'fawq' meaning superiority in rank and power (with examples from Quranic verses about knowledge, power, and authority). Second, 'fawq' meaning spatial height (with the example of 'what is above it' meaning greater in smallness).
Notice that the second example is non-spatial but uses 'fawq' for an abstract increase.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Razi ends this section with a question: since the word can mean both, why do they insist on the spatial meaning? Think about how this sets up the next proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذي ذكروه ثانياً وهو التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على ذكر الفوقية . فجوابه : أن لفظ الفوق يستعمل في الرتبة والقدرة . فقد قال الله تعالى : ( وفوق كل ذي علم عليم - وإنا فوقهم قادرون يد الله فوق أيديهم والمراد بالفوقية في هذه الآيات : الفوقية بالقهر والقدرة ) ويستعمل فى الفوق ، الذي بمعنى الجهة قال تعالى : بعوضة فما فوقها أي أزيد منها في صفة الصغر والحقارة . وإذا كان لفظ الفوق محتملا للفوق فى الرتبة ، والفوق في الجهة ، فلم حملوه على الفوق في الجهة ؟ والذي يدل على أن المراد بلفظ الفوق ههنا : الفوق بالقدرة والمكنة وجوه : الأول : إنه قال : « وهو القاهر فوق عباده والفوقية المقرونة بالقهر ، هى الفوقية بالقدرة والمكنة ، لا بمعنى الجهة . بدليل : أن الحارس قد يكون فوق السلطان فى الجهة ، ولا يقال إنه فوق السلطان
As for what they mentioned secondly, which is clinging to verses that include mention of 'above' [al-fawqiyya], its answer is that the word 'above' [al-fawq] is used for rank and power. For God Almighty said: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' and 'And We are above them, dominant' and 'The hand of God is above their hands' — and what is meant by 'above' in these verses is superiority by force and power. And it is used for 'above' in the sense of direction. He said: 'a mosquito or what is above it' meaning larger in smallness and contemptibility. And since the word 'above' can indicate superiority in rank and superiority in direction, why did they interpret it as superiority in direction? That which indicates that what is meant by the word 'above' here is superiority in power and capability are several points: first, He said: 'And He is the Supreme [al-Qahir] above His servants,' and 'above' conjoined with force indicates superiority in power and capability, not in the sense of direction. Evidence: the guard may be above the sultan in direction, yet it is not said that he is above the sultan.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that verses mentioning 'above' (fawq) prove God is spatially above. The author first notes that 'fawq' is used in two senses: (1) superiority in rank/power, and (2) spatial height. He cites several Quranic verses where the meaning is clearly rank or power: e.g., 'above every knower is the All-Knowing' (superior knowledge), 'We are above them, dominant' (power), 'God's hand is above their hands' (authority). He also gives an example of the second meaning: 'or what is above it' meaning larger in smallness—a clear non-spatial use. Then he poses a rhetorical question: why interpret it as spatial direction when it could mean superiority? He then provides a decisive proof: the verse 'He is al-Qahir above His servants' links fawq with qahr (compulsion/dominance). Since qahr implies power, the fawq here must be power, not direction. He adds an analogy: a guard may be physically above a sultan in position, but is not said to be 'above' him in rank; this demonstrates that fawq when combined with qahr indicates power.

Key Terms
فوق
قهر
جهة
رتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'fawq' in the Quran always refers to physical direction or location.
Correction: Razi shows that 'fawq' is used for both rank/power and direction, and in many verses it clearly means power.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Razi give as an example of 'fawq' meaning direction?
A) 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing'
B) 'And We are above them, dominant'
C) 'A mosquito or what is above it'
D) 'The hand of God is above their hands'

Key Takeaway
'Fawq' is used in the Quran for both rank/power and direction, so it cannot automatically be taken as spatial when referring to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses describing Allah as 'above', does a mental image of physical height come to your mind? How does Razi's analysis challenge that instinct, and what does it mean for how you truly conceive of Allah's transcendence?

?
Razi questions why opponents insist on the spatial interpretation of 'fawq' for Allah. In your own heart, do you find yourself attached to tangible images of Allah because they feel safer? Are you willing to let go of that and embrace a more transcendent, awe-inspiring concept of Allah—even if it feels less comfortable? Why or why not?

Why 'Fawq' in 'Al-Qahir Fawqa Ibadihi' Means Power, Not Direction
Razi provides a decisive proof from the conjunction of 'fawq' with 'qahr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'He is al-Qahir above His servants' (Qur'an 6:18) must refer to God's power and not to a spatial location.

Core Idea
Fawq paired with qahr proves divine power, not physical height.

The most compelling evidence that 'fawq' in reference to God means power is found in the verse where it is combined with 'qahr' (compelling force). Imam Razi explains this connection and provides a simple analogy to clarify the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage where Razi gives the first of several proofs that 'fawq' in this verse means power.
The passage cites the verse and then gives a logical argument followed by an analogy.
2
Identify the key verse and the conjunction
Notice the verse 'Huwa al-Qahir fawqa ibadihi'. Razi points out that 'fawq' is linked with 'qahr', which means force or dominance.
The conjunction is crucial: 'al-fawqiyya al-maghruna bil-qahr'.
3
Understand the logical inference
When 'fawq' is paired with 'qahr', it must indicate superiority in power, not location, because 'qahr' inherently implies power.
4
Reflect on the guard analogy
Razi gives an analogy: a guard may be physically above a sultan (e.g., on a balcony), but no one says the guard is 'above' the sultan in rank. This shows that mere physical height does not convey superiority.
The analogy proves that 'fawq' with 'qahr' cannot be spatial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذي ذكروه ثانياً وهو التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على ذكر الفوقية . فجوابه : أن لفظ الفوق يستعمل في الرتبة والقدرة . فقد قال الله تعالى : ( وفوق كل ذي علم عليم - وإنا فوقهم قادرون يد الله فوق أيديهم والمراد بالفوقية في هذه الآيات : الفوقية بالقهر والقدرة ) ويستعمل فى الفوق ، الذي بمعنى الجهة قال تعالى : بعوضة فما فوقها أي أزيد منها في صفة الصغر والحقارة . وإذا كان لفظ الفوق محتملا للفوق فى الرتبة ، والفوق في الجهة ، فلم حملوه على الفوق في الجهة ؟ والذي يدل على أن المراد بلفظ الفوق ههنا : الفوق بالقدرة والمكنة وجوه : الأول : إنه قال : « وهو القاهر فوق عباده والفوقية المقرونة بالقهر ، هى الفوقية بالقدرة والمكنة ، لا بمعنى الجهة . بدليل : أن الحارس قد يكون فوق السلطان فى الجهة ، ولا يقال إنه فوق السلطان
As for what they mentioned secondly, which is clinging to verses that include mention of 'above' [al-fawqiyya], its answer is that the word 'above' [al-fawq] is used for rank and power. For God Almighty said: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' and 'And We are above them, dominant' and 'The hand of God is above their hands' — and what is meant by 'above' in these verses is superiority by force and power. And it is used for 'above' in the sense of direction. He said: 'a mosquito or what is above it' meaning larger in smallness and contemptibility. And since the word 'above' can indicate superiority in rank and superiority in direction, why did they interpret it as superiority in direction? That which indicates that what is meant by the word 'above' here is superiority in power and capability are several points: first, He said: 'And He is the Supreme [al-Qahir] above His servants,' and 'above' conjoined with force indicates superiority in power and capability, not in the sense of direction. Evidence: the guard may be above the sultan in direction, yet it is not said that he is above the sultan.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that verses mentioning 'above' (fawq) prove God is spatially above. The author first notes that 'fawq' is used in two senses: (1) superiority in rank/power, and (2) spatial height. He cites several Quranic verses where the meaning is clearly rank or power: e.g., 'above every knower is the All-Knowing' (superior knowledge), 'We are above them, dominant' (power), 'God's hand is above their hands' (authority). He also gives an example of the second meaning: 'or what is above it' meaning larger in smallness—a clear non-spatial use. Then he poses a rhetorical question: why interpret it as spatial direction when it could mean superiority? He then provides a decisive proof: the verse 'He is al-Qahir above His servants' links fawq with qahr (compulsion/dominance). Since qahr implies power, the fawq here must be power, not direction. He adds an analogy: a guard may be physically above a sultan in position, but is not said to be 'above' him in rank; this demonstrates that fawq when combined with qahr indicates power.

Key Terms
فوق
قهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'al-Qahir fawqa ibadihi' proves that God is spatially above His servants.
Correction: The verse pairs 'fawq' with 'qahr', which indicates power, not direction. The guard analogy shows spatial height does not imply superiority.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Razi use to show that 'fawq' with 'qahr' does not mean spatial direction?
A) A king on his throne
B) A guard above a sultan
C) A bird flying above
D) A mountain above a valley

Key Takeaway
When 'fawq' is coupled with 'qahr' in the Quran, it necessarily means superiority in power, not spatial direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Akbar' (God is Greater), do you ever unconsciously picture God as being 'up there' in a spatial sense? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what must you change in your heart to truly affirm that His greatness is beyond physical dimensions?
?
If you truly believed that God's 'fawq' means absolute power over you in every moment, would you still rebel against His commands in the areas of your life where you feel 'in control'? What specific area today requires you to submit to His 'qahr'?
?
The analogy shows that physical height does not imply superiority. Is there anyone you consider 'beneath you' because of their social status or abilities? How does realizing that God alone is truly 'above' reshape your treatment of others?

The Mujassim's Defense Against Tashbih
Al-Razi presents the mujassim's defense based on essential difference and Qur'anic parallels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mujassim’s defense against the charge of tashbih and their use of Qur'anic similarities as evidence.

Core Idea
The mujassim argue essential difference prevents tashbih.

This lesson examines the argument of the anthropomorphists (mujassima) who claim that despite affirming God as a body, they are not guilty of tashbih because they believe God is unlike all creation in essence. They cite Qur'anic verses where similar attributes are applied to God and humans without implying full likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the mujassim's claim
Read the passage in Arabic and translation to understand the core argument: essential difference prevents tashbih.
Notice they first assert a distinction in essence.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three examples of attributes shared between God and humans in the Qur'an: hearing/seeing, eyes, hands.
Each example pairs a divine and human usage.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the mujassim think these examples support their position and how this challenges the standard view of tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: في أن المجسم هل يوصف بأنه مشبه أم لا ؟ .
قال المجسم : إنا وإن [؟قلنا] : إنه تعالى جسم مختص بالحيز والجهة إلا أنا نعتقد : أنه بخلاف سائر الأجسام في ذاته وحقيقته . وذلك يمنع من القول بالتشبيه. فإن إثبات المساواة فى الصور لا يوجب إثبات التشبيه ، ويدل عليه : أنه تعالى صرح في كتابه بالمساواة في الصفات الكثيرة ، ولم يقل أحد بأن ذلك يوجب التشبيه .
فالأول : قال في صفة نفسه : « إنني معكما : أسمع وأرى » ، وقال في صفة الإنسان : « فجعلناه سميعا بصيرا » :
الثاني : قال تعالى : « واصنع الفلك بأعيننا » ، وقال في الإنسان : « ترى أعينهم تفيض من الدمع » .
الثالث : قال تعالى : « بل يداه مبسوطتان » ، وفي الإنسان : « ذلك بما قدمت يداك » وقال في نفسه : « عما عملت أيدينا [؟أنعاما] » وفي الإنسان : « يد الله فوق أيديهم » .
Chapter Two: On whether the anthropomorphist (mujassim) is described as an assimilationist (mushabbih) or not.
The anthropomorphist said: 'Indeed, even though we say that He, exalted, is a body specific to space and direction, yet we believe that He is different from all other bodies in His essence and reality. And that prevents the assertion of tashbih (assimilation). For the affirmation of equality in forms does not necessitate the affirmation of tashbih. Evidence for it is that He, exalted, stated explicitly in His Book equality in many attributes, and no one said that that necessitates tashbih.'
First: He said about His own attribute: 'Indeed, I am with you: I hear and I see' (20:46), and He said about the human attribute: 'Then We made him hearing and seeing' (76:2).
Second: He, exalted, said: 'And construct the ship under Our eyes' (11:37), and He said about humans: 'You see their eyes overflowing with tears' (5:83).
Third: He, exalted, said: 'But both His hands are outstretched' (5:64), and about humans: 'That is because of what your hands have sent forth' (22:10), and He said about Himself: 'Of what Our hands have created [cattle]' (36:71), and about humans: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' (48:10).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the anthropomorphists (mujassima) in defense of their position. They claim that despite affirming that God is a body occupying space and direction, they maintain that His essence is entirely unlike other bodies. This essential difference, they argue, prevents the charge of tashbih (comparing God to creation). They support this by noting that mere similarity in certain descriptions does not amount to full likeness, and they cite the Qur'an as evidence: God attributes to Himself attributes like hearing, seeing, eyes, and hands, and also attributes similar terms to humans, yet no one considers those verses as implying tashbih. The author then lists three pairs of examples: (1) hearing and seeing attributed to God and humans; (2) 'eyes' attributed to God (in the sense of care) and to humans; (3) 'hands' attributed to God and humans. The implication is that the mujassim see their own position as consistent with the Qur'anic method of attribution without falling into outright anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
مجسم (mujassim)
مشبه (mushabbih)
تشبيه (tashbih)
مساواة في الصور
يد
عين
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mujassim see no difference between God and creation; they affirm essential difference.
Correction: The mujassim explicitly state that God is different in essence from all bodies, and that similarity in descriptions does not amount to tashbih.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the mujassim use to argue that they are not guilty of tashbih?
A) They deny all divine attributes.
B) They claim that God is unlike all bodies in essence, despite affirming bodily attributes.
C) They reject the Qur'anic verses about God's attributes.
D) They say that only hands and eyes are attributed to God, not hearing or seeing.

Key Takeaway
The mujassim defend their position by asserting an essential difference and using Qur'anic parallels to argue that shared descriptions do not imply full likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'anic verses that attribute hands, eyes, or hearing to Allah, do you instinctively feel a need to reinterpret or accept them literally? How does that reaction reveal your own assumptions about God's nature?
?
The mujassim claim to affirm essential difference, yet you might find their position uncomfortable. What is the source of that discomfort in your heart—is it concern for God's transcendence or a fear of being associated with a condemned sect?
?
If someone today accused you of tashbih for holding a particular belief about God's attributes, would you be able to defend yourself with the same level of scriptural evidence the mujassim used? Why or why not?

Divine Names Al-Hakam, Ar-Rahim, Ash-Shakur, and Al-Ali
Razi lists four divine names with Quranic proofs and notes on human usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural basis for the divine names Al-Hakam, Ar-Rahim, Ash-Shakur, and Al-Ali, and how some are shared with human beings in a limited sense.

Core Idea
Divine names are rooted in revelation, and some are shared with creation.

Imam al-Razi continues his enumeration of the beautiful names of Allah by presenting four more names with their Quranic proofs. This lesson covers the tenth to thirteenth names, where we see that while some names apply exclusively to Allah, others are used for humans as well, reminding us of the distinction between the Creator and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the four names carefully. Notice the pattern: each name is stated, followed by a Quranic verse.
Pay attention to the note about human usage for Al-Ali.
2
Understand each name
For Al-Hakam, note the two verses: one for divine judgment, one for human arbitrators. For Ar-Rahim, note the brief comment 'and this is apparent.' For Ash-Shakur, the verse shows appreciation. For Al-Ali, the name is used for humans as a given name.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask why Razi mentions human usage for Al-Ali. It shows that some names are shared, but the divine meaning is absolute and perfect.
This illustrates an important principle in understanding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: « الحكم » قال الله: « ألا له الحكم » ووصفنا به فقال: « فابعثوا حكما من أهله وحكما من أهلها ». الحادي عشر: الراحم والرحيم. وهذا ظاهر. الثاني عشر: الشكور قال: « إن ربنا لغفور شكور ». الثالث عشر: العلى والإنسان يسمى نفسه بذلك. ومنه: «على» رضي الله عنه. الرابع عشر: الكبير قال عن [؟] وهو العملى [؟] الكبير نفسه: وقال: « إن له أبا شيخا كبير » وقال حكاية عن المرأتين: « وأبونا شيخ كبير ». الخامس عشر: الحكيم والله تعالى وصف نفسه في كتابه به فقال: « تنزيل من حكيم حميد ». السادس عشر: الشهيد قال في حق الخلق: « فكيف إذا جئنا من كل أمة بشهيد » وقال في نفسه: « أو لم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ». السابع عشر: الحق قال: « فتعالى الله الملك الحق » وبالحق أنزلناه وبالحق نزل. الثامن عشر: الوكيل. قال الله تعالى: « وهو على كل شيء وكيل » وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك، فيقال: فلان وكيل فلان. التاسع عشر: المولى. قال تعالى: « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم » ثم قال في حقنا: « ولكل جعلنا مولى » والتي قال: « من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه ». العشرون: الولى. قال الله تعالى: « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله والذين آمنوا » وقال التي: « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل » وقال تعالى: « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ». الحادي والعشرون: الحى. قال تعالى: « هو الحى لا إله إلا هو » ألم الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم، وقال: « وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي ».
The tenth: 'Al-Hakam (The Judge).' Allah said: 'Indeed, judgement is His.' And He described us by it, saying: 'Send a judge from his family and a judge from her family.' The eleventh: Ar-Rahim and Ar-Rahim. And this is apparent. The twelfth: Ash-Shakur (The Appreciative). He said: 'Indeed, our Lord is Forgiving, Appreciative.' The thirteenth: Al-Ali (The Most High). And a man may call himself by that. Among it: 'Ali' – may Allah be pleased with him. The fourteenth: Al-Kabir (The Great). He said concerning [someone?] and he is the blind [؟], the great himself: And He said: 'Indeed, he has an old father' and He related from the two women: 'Our father is an old man.' The fifteenth: Al-Hakim (The Wise). And Allah the Exalted described Himself in His Book by it, saying: 'A revelation from the Wise, the Praiseworthy.' The sixteenth: Ash-Shahid (The Witness). He said concerning creation: 'So how will it be when we bring from every nation a witness?' And He said concerning Himself: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' The seventeenth: Al-Haqq (The Truth). He said: 'So exalted is Allah, the Sovereign, the Truth.' And with the truth We sent it down, and with the truth it descended. The eighteenth: Al-Wakil (The Disposer of Affairs). Allah the Exalted said: 'And He is over all things a Disposer.' And creation may be described with that, so it is said: So-and-so is the agent of so-and-so. The nineteenth: Al-Mawla (The Protector). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Protector of those who believe, and the disbelievers have no protector.' Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have appointed heirs' and the Prophet said: 'Whomever I am his master, Ali is his master.' The twentieth: Al-Wali (The Ally). Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your ally is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe.' And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid.' And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies of one another.' The twenty-first: Al-Hayy (The Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' – 'Alif Lam Meem. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Ever-Living, the Sustainer of existence.' And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve divine names (from the tenth to the twenty-first) from the Islamic tradition of al-asmā' al-ḥusnā. For each name, Imam al-Rāzī provides a Qur'anic proof text and often notes that the same name can be applied to human beings in a limited sense.

The structure is consistent: the name is stated, then a relevant Qur'anic verse is quoted. Sometimes additional clarification is given. For example, for 'al-Ḥakam' (The Judge), he cites verse 6:62 and also notes that the term 'ḥakam' is used for human arbitrators in 4:35. For 'ar-Rāḥim' and 'ar-Raḥīm' (The Compassionate and the Merciful), he simply says 'and this is apparent,' meaning the meaning is clear. For 'al-'Alī' (The Most High), he remarks that humans also use that name (e.g., the Companion 'Alī). For 'al-Kabīr' (The Great), the text appears corrupted but cites verses about an old father (12:78, 28:23). For 'al-Ḥakīm' (The Wise), he quotes 41:42. For 'ash-Shahīd' (The Witness), he uses 4:41 and 41:53. For 'al-Ḥaqq' (The Truth), he uses 20:114 and 17:105. For 'al-Wakīl' (The Disposer), he uses 6:102 and notes that a human agent is also called 'wakīl'. For 'al-Mawlā' (The Protector), he uses 47:11, 4:33, and a hadith about 'Alī. For 'al-Walī' (The Ally), he uses 5:55, a hadith about marriage guardian, and 9:71. For 'al-Ḥayy' (The Living), he uses 40:65, 3:2, and 21:30.

This passage functions as a concise scriptural proof for each name, demonstrating that they are divinely revealed and that some are shared with creation in a derivative sense. The author does not explore theological implications here but simply compiles the evidence.

Key Terms
الحكم
الراحم
الرحيم
الشكور
العلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All these names are exclusively divine and cannot be used for humans.
Correction: While the divine names are perfect and absolute, some like Al-Hakam and Al-Ali can be applied to humans in a limited, derivative manner, as the passage shows.

Checkpoint
Which divine name is supported by the verse 'And He described us by it, saying: Send a judge from his family and a judge from her family'?
A) Al-Ali
B) Al-Hakam
C) Ash-Shakur
D) Ar-Rahim

Key Takeaway
The names Al-Hakam, Ar-Rahim, Ash-Shakur, and Al-Ali are scripturally established, and some are shared with humans in a limited sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone called 'judge' or 'the compassionate one,' does your heart immediately remember that these attributes belong fully and perfectly to Allah? If not, what would it take to train your heart to see His attributes behind every human reflection?
?
The name 'Ash-Shakur' tells us that Allah appreciates even the smallest good deed. Are you more focused on the weight of your sins than on seeking His appreciation through sincere acts? Will you today perform one small deed solely hoping for His shukr?
?
You may share the name 'Ali' or another divine name, but do you live with the awareness that only Allah is Al-Ali in absolute sense? How does that awareness change the way you view your own status and worth?
