Divine Names Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid
Razi lists three divine names with Quranic proofs, including human usage examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic proofs for the divine names Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid, and how they are used in the Quran for both Allah and creation.

Core Idea
Even names that appear in human contexts point to Allah's unique perfection.

Imam al-Razi continues with the fourteenth to sixteenth divine names: Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid. Each is accompanied by Quranic verses that sometimes also refer to human beings, highlighting the distinction between the Creator's absolute attributes and their limited manifestations in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the three names. Note the structure: name, then verses, and sometimes a note.
The text for Al-Kabir has some unclear words, but the verses are clear.
2
Examine each name
For Al-Kabir, the verses refer to an old father (used for humans). For Al-Hakim, a direct divine attribution. For Ash-Shahid, two verses: one about human witnesses, one about Allah as witness.
Notice the pattern of dual usage.
3
Consider the theological lesson
Why does Razi include verses that describe humans? It shows that the root meanings are shared, but Allah's attributes are absolute and unparalleled.

For 'ash-Shahīd' (The Witness), he uses 4:41 and 41:53. For 'al-Ḥaqq' (The Truth), he uses 20:114 and 17:105. For 'al-Wakīl' (The Disposer), he uses 6:102 and notes that a human agent is also called 'wakīl'. For 'al-Mawlā' (The Protector), he uses 47:11, 4:33, and a hadith about 'Alī. For 'al-Walī' (The Ally), he uses 5:55, a hadith about marriage guardian, and 9:71. For 'al-Ḥayy' (The Living), he uses 40:65, 3:2, and 21:30.

This passage functions as a concise scriptural proof for each name, demonstrating that they are divinely revealed and that some are shared with creation in a derivative sense. The author does not explore theological implications here but simply compiles the evidence.

Key Terms
الكبير
الحكيم
الشهيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These names are only used for Allah in the Quran.
Correction: The Quran uses 'kabir' and 'shahid' for humans as well, but in a limited sense. Allah's attributes are perfect and absolute.

Checkpoint
Which divine name is supported by the verse 'A revelation from the Wise, the Praiseworthy'?
A) Al-Kabir
B) Al-Hakim
C) Ash-Shahid
D) Al-Hakam

Key Takeaway
Al-Kabir, Al-Hakim, and Ash-Shahid are divine names with Quranic proofs, and their roots appear in human contexts, highlighting divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is the Greatest), do you feel the weight of His absolute greatness in your daily decisions, or has it become a hollow phrase? Will you today pause before each prayer and truly magnify Him in your heart?
?
Allah is Ash-Shahid, witnessing your every thought and action. Is there a secret sin you are comfortable with because you think no one sees? How would your behavior change if you lived every moment aware that the Witness is always present? Commit now to a specific step toward accountability.
?
The name Al-Hakim reminds you that Allah's wisdom is perfect, even when your life feels chaotic. Is there a situation where you are resisting His decree because it seems unfair? Will you today submit that matter to His wisdom and trust that He knows what you do not?

Divine Names Al-Haqq, Al-Wakil, and Al-Mawla
Razi lists three divine names with Quranic proofs and human usage examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic proofs for the divine names Al-Haqq, Al-Wakil, and Al-Mawla, and how some are used for humans in specific contexts.

Core Idea
Truth, Disposal, and Protection belong to Allah, but humans share these roles in a limited way.

The seventeenth to nineteenth divine names are Al-Haqq, Al-Wakil, and Al-Mawla. Imam al-Razi provides Quranic verses that establish these names for Allah, and he also notes that the same terms are used for human beings in specific cultural or legal contexts, such as agents and guardians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the three names. Notice the pattern: name, verse, and sometimes a human usage example.
For Al-Mawla, there is also a hadith.
2
Examine each name
For Al-Haqq, two verses: one about divine truth and one about revelation. For Al-Wakil, a verse about Allah as disposer, and a note about human agents. For Al-Mawla, verses about Allah as protector and a hadith about Ali.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does Razi include human usage? It helps distinguish between the Creator's absolute attributes and created beings' limited roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: « الحكم » قال الله: « ألا له الحكم » ووصفنا به فقال: « فابعثوا حكما من أهله وحكما من أهلها ». الحادي عشر: الراحم والرحيم. وهذا ظاهر. الثاني عشر: الشكور قال: « إن ربنا لغفور شكور ». الثالث عشر: العلى والإنسان يسمى نفسه بذلك. ومنه: «على» رضي الله عنه. الرابع عشر: الكبير قال عن [؟] وهو العملى [؟] الكبير نفسه: وقال: « إن له أبا شيخا كبير » وقال حكاية عن المرأتين: « وأبونا شيخ كبير ». الخامس عشر: الحكيم والله تعالى وصف نفسه في كتابه به فقال: « تنزيل من حكيم حميد ». السادس عشر: الشهيد قال في حق الخلق: « فكيف إذا جئنا من كل أمة بشهيد » وقال في نفسه: « أو لم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ». السابع عشر: الحق قال: « فتعالى الله الملك الحق » وبالحق أنزلناه وبالحق نزل. الثامن عشر: الوكيل. قال الله تعالى: « وهو على كل شيء وكيل » وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك، فيقال: فلان وكيل فلان. التاسع عشر: المولى. قال تعالى: « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم » ثم قال في حقنا: « ولكل جعلنا مولى » والتي قال: « من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه ». العشرون: الولى. قال الله تعالى: « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله والذين آمنوا » وقال التي: « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل » وقال تعالى: « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ». الحادي والعشرون: الحى. قال تعالى: « هو الحى لا إله إلا هو » ألم الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم، وقال: « وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي ».
The tenth: 'Al-Hakam (The Judge).' Allah said: 'Indeed, judgement is His.' And He described us by it, saying: 'Send a judge from his family and a judge from her family.' The eleventh: Ar-Rahim and Ar-Rahim. And this is apparent. The twelfth: Ash-Shakur (The Appreciative). He said: 'Indeed, our Lord is Forgiving, Appreciative.' The thirteenth: Al-Ali (The Most High). And a man may call himself by that. Among it: 'Ali' – may Allah be pleased with him. The fourteenth: Al-Kabir (The Great). He said concerning [someone?] and he is the blind [؟], the great himself: And He said: 'Indeed, he has an old father' and He related from the two women: 'Our father is an old man.' The fifteenth: Al-Hakim (The Wise). And Allah the Exalted described Himself in His Book by it, saying: 'A revelation from the Wise, the Praiseworthy.' The sixteenth: Ash-Shahid (The Witness). He said concerning creation: 'So how will it be when we bring from every nation a witness?' And He said concerning Himself: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' The seventeenth: Al-Haqq (The Truth). He said: 'So exalted is Allah, the Sovereign, the Truth.' And with the truth We sent it down, and with the truth it descended. The eighteenth: Al-Wakil (The Disposer of Affairs). Allah the Exalted said: 'And He is over all things a Disposer.' And creation may be described with that, so it is said: So-and-so is the agent of so-and-so. The nineteenth: Al-Mawla (The Protector). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Protector of those who believe, and the disbelievers have no protector.' Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have appointed heirs' and the Prophet said: 'Whomever I am his master, Ali is his master.' The twentieth: Al-Wali (The Ally). Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, your ally is Allah and His Messenger and those who believe.' And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian, her marriage is invalid.' And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies of one another.' The twenty-first: Al-Hayy (The Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' – 'Alif Lam Meem. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Ever-Living, the Sustainer of existence.' And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve divine names (from the tenth to the twenty-first) from the Islamic tradition of al-asmā' al-ḥusnā. For each name, Imam al-Rāzī provides a Qur'anic proof text and often notes that the same name can be applied to human beings in a limited sense.

The structure is consistent: the name is stated, then a relevant Qur'anic verse is quoted. Sometimes additional clarification is given. For example, for 'al-Ḥakam' (The Judge), he cites verse 6:62 and also notes that the term 'ḥakam' is used for human arbitrators in 4:35. For 'ar-Rāḥim' and 'ar-Raḥīm' (The Compassionate and the Merciful), he simply says 'and this is apparent,' meaning the meaning is clear. For 'al-'Alī' (The Most High), he remarks that humans also use that name (e.g., the Companion 'Alī). For 'al-Kabīr' (The Great), the text appears corrupted but cites verses about an old father (12:78, 28:23). For 'al-Ḥakīm' (The Wise), he quotes 41:42. For 'ash-Shahīd' (The Witness), he uses 4:41 and 41:53. For 'al-Ḥaqq' (The Truth), he uses 20:114 and 17:105. For 'al-Wakīl' (The Disposer), he uses 6:102 and notes that a human agent is also called 'wakīl'. For 'al-Mawlā' (The Protector), he uses 47:11, 4:33, and a hadith about 'Alī. For 'al-Walī' (The Ally), he uses 5:55, a hadith about marriage guardian, and 9:71. For 'al-Ḥayy' (The Living), he uses 40:65, 3:2, and 21:30.

This passage functions as a concise scriptural proof for each name, demonstrating that they are divinely revealed and that some are shared with creation in a derivative sense. The author does not explore theological implications here but simply compiles the evidence.

Key Terms
الحق
الوكيل
المولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These names are never used for humans in the Quran or hadith.
Correction: The passage shows that 'Wakil' and 'Mawla' are used for humans, but Allah's dispensation and protection are absolute.

Checkpoint
Which divine name is supported by the verse 'He is over all things a Disposer'?
A) Al-Haqq
B) Al-Wakil
C) Al-Mawla
D) Al-Hakam

Key Takeaway
Al-Haqq, Al-Wakil, and Al-Mawla are divine names with Quranic proofs, and their human usage underscores Allah's uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as Al-Haqq, do you truly live seeking the truth in every matter? What lie or falsehood are you tolerating in your life right now that contradicts His name?
?
You trust people to handle your affairs, calling them 'wakil'. But have you entrusted your most critical matter—your salvation—to anyone besides Al-Wakil? What keeps you from full reliance on Him?
?
The Prophet said 'Whomever I am his master, Ali is his master.' Do you feel the weight of that allegiance? Who or what is your real mawla in the decisions you make daily?

Divine Names Al-Wali and Al-Hayy
Razi lists two divine names with Quranic proofs and human usage examples.

Learning Objective

Core Idea

In this final lesson from the sequence, Imam al-Razi presents the twentieth and twenty-first divine names: Al-Wali and Al-Hayy. Each is supported by multiple Quranic verses, and for Al-Wali, he also includes a hadith and a verse about human guardians and allies, demonstrating the shared linguistic root.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

Examine each name

Al-Wakil: The Trustee
Razi presents al-Wakil with Quranic proof and creaturely usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Wakil is a divine name proven by the Quran, and that the term also applies to creatures in a limited, metaphorical sense without implying similarity to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is the absolute Trustee over all things; human trusteeship is derived and limited.

Razi begins the eighteenth divine name, al-Wakil, by citing a Quranic verse that establishes Allah as the Trustee over all things. He then notes that the same term is applied to creatures, but this does not imply any similarity in the essence of the attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the name, then the Quranic proof, then the creaturely application.
The verse is from Surah al-An'am (6:102).
2
Identify the Two Uses
Observe that the term 'wakil' is used for Allah in an absolute sense and for creatures in a limited sense. Mark the difference in context.
3
Reflect on the Distinction
Ask yourself: Why is it important to recognize that the same Arabic word can apply to Allah and creation without implying similarity?
This guard against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن عشر : الوكيل. قال الله تعالى : وهو على كل شيء وكيل (٣٦) وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك ، فيقال : فلان وكيل فلان .
التاسع عشر : المولى . قال تعالى : « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا ، وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم (۳۷)، ثم قال في حقنا : « ولكل جعلنا مولى (۳۸) والتي قال : « من كنت مولاه ، فعلي مولاه (۳۹) ،
العشرون : الولى . قال الله تعالى : « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله ، والذين آمنوا (٤٠) ، وقال التي : « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل (٤١) ، وقال تعالى : « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض (٤٢) ،
الحادي والعشرون: الحى . قال تعالى: «هو الحى لا إله إلا هو (٤٣) - ألم . . الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم (٤٤) وقال : وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي (٤٥) ،
(٣٦) الأنعام ١٠٢ (۳۷) محمد ۱۱ (٣٨) النساء ۳۳ (۳۹) أحاديث الفرق هي أحاديث آحاد (٤٠) المائدة ٥٥ (٤١) أنظر مناقب الشافعى لفخر الدين الرازی - طبع الكايات الأزهرية (٤٢) التوبة ٧١ (٤٣) غافر ٦٥ (٤٤) أول آل عمران (٤٥) الأنبياء ٣٠
Eighteenth: al-Wakil (the Trustee). Allah Most High said: 'And He is over all things a Trustee (Wakil)' (36). And creatures are described with it, so it is said: 'So-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so.'
Nineteenth: al-Mawla (the Patron). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Patron (Mawla) of those who believe, and that the disbelievers have no patron' (37). Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have made heirs (mawla)' (38). And the Prophet said: 'For whomever I am his patron (mawla), 'Ali is his patron' (39).
Twentieth: al-Wali (the Ally). Allah Most High said: 'Your ally (wali) is only Allah and His Messenger and those who believe' (40). And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian (wali), her marriage is invalid' (41). And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies (awliya') of one another' (42).
Twenty-first: al-Hayy (the Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' (43) - 'Alif Lam Mim. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Living, the Self-Subsisting' (44). And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing' (45).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four divine names (al-asmā’ al-ḥusnā) in sequence, each with Quranic proof and often a note on how the same term applies to creation. Razi follows the same pattern for each entry: first he states the name, then he quotes one or more Quranic verses that establish it for Allah, and finally he shows that the term is also used for creatures, but in a limited or metaphorical sense.

For al-Wakīl (the Trustee), the verse from al-An‘ām 6:102 proves that Allah is over all things a Wakīl. Then Razi notes that creatures are described with this word, as in 'so-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so', but the meaning for Allah is unique and absolute.

For al-Mawlā (the Patron), he cites Muḥammad 47:11 stating that Allah is the Mawlā of believers, while disbelievers have no mawlā. He then mentions the verse from al-Nisā’ 4:33 which uses mawlā for human heirs, and the famous hadith 'man kuntu mawlāhu fa-‘Aliyyun mawlāhu', though he notes the hadith is āḥād (singular transmission). This shows the term applies to humans as well, but divine mawlā is superior.

For al-Walī (the Ally), Razi uses al-Mā’idah 5:55 to show Allah and the Messenger are the believers' walī. Then he cites a hadith about the necessity of a guardian for marriage, and al-Tawbah 9:71 where believers are awliyā’ of each other. Thus the term is shared, but the meaning differs: Allah's walī is unique and absolute.

For al-Ḥayy (the Living), he gives Āl ‘Imrān 3:2 and Ghāfir 40:65 asserting that Allah is the Living, and then al-Anbiyā’ 21:30 saying 'We made from water every living thing' for creatures, showing that created life is derived and limited.

This passage demonstrates Razi's method: he defends the divine names by scripture while acknowledging that the same Arabic words are used for contingent beings, but without any similarity in essence.

Key Terms
الوكيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a name applies to both Allah and creation, then the attribute is shared in the same sense.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the term 'wakil' is used for both, but the context shows that Allah's trusteeship is absolute and over all things, whereas human trusteeship is limited and metaphorical.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Razi cite as proof for the divine name al-Wakil?
A) 'He is the Living' (Ghafir 65)
B) 'And He is over all things a Trustee' (al-An'am 102)
C) 'Allah is the Patron of those who believe' (Muhammad 11)
D) 'Your ally is only Allah' (al-Ma'idah 55)

Key Takeaway
Allah is uniquely and absolutely al-Wakil, the Trustee over all things, while creatures use the term in a finite sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly entrust all your affairs—your health, wealth, relationships, and future—to Allah as the absolute Wakil, or do you rely on human agents as if they have independent control? How can you practice that trust in a specific decision you're facing today?
?
When you delegate a task to someone else, do you remember that their trusteeship is derived from Allah and limited, and that Allah is the ultimate Trustee over that matter? How does that change the way you assign responsibility and manage worry?
?
The verse says Allah is 'over all things a Trustee.' Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are the wakil instead of Allah? What would it look like to surrender that area to Him starting now?

Al-Mawla: The Patron
Razi lists al-Mawla with Quranic proofs and a hadith, plus creaturely application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Mawla is a divine name meaning the Patron and Protector, with Quranic and hadith evidence, and that the term also applies to human relationships.

Core Idea
Allah is the true Patron of believers; human patronage is derivative and limited.

Razi moves to the nineteenth name, al-Mawla, citing a verse that distinguishes believers who have Allah as their Mawla from disbelievers who have none. He then shows the term's human usage through a Quranic verse and a prophetic hadith, underscoring the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Mawla. Note the three citations: one Quranic verse for Allah, one for humans, and a hadith.
The hadith is classified as ahad (singular transmission) by Razi.
2
Analyze the Evidence
Compare the divine usage (believers' Mawla) with the human usage (heirs in inheritance) and the Prophet's statement about 'Ali.
3
Understand the Hierarchy
Reflect on how the same word carries different levels of meaning: absolute for Allah, contingent for humans.
This illustrates the principle of tawhid in names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن عشر : الوكيل. قال الله تعالى : وهو على كل شيء وكيل (٣٦) وقد يوصف الخلق بذلك ، فيقال : فلان وكيل فلان .
التاسع عشر : المولى . قال تعالى : « ذلك بأن الله مولى الذين آمنوا ، وأن الكافرين لا مولى لهم (۳۷)، ثم قال في حقنا : « ولكل جعلنا مولى (۳۸) والتي قال : « من كنت مولاه ، فعلي مولاه (۳۹) ،
العشرون : الولى . قال الله تعالى : « إنما وليكم الله ورسوله ، والذين آمنوا (٤٠) ، وقال التي : « أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحها باطل (٤١) ، وقال تعالى : « والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض (٤٢) ،
الحادي والعشرون: الحى . قال تعالى: «هو الحى لا إله إلا هو (٤٣) - ألم . . الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم (٤٤) وقال : وجعلنا من الماء كل شيء حي (٤٥) ،
(٣٦) الأنعام ١٠٢ (۳۷) محمد ۱۱ (٣٨) النساء ۳۳ (۳۹) أحاديث الفرق هي أحاديث آحاد (٤٠) المائدة ٥٥ (٤١) أنظر مناقب الشافعى لفخر الدين الرازی - طبع الكايات الأزهرية (٤٢) التوبة ٧١ (٤٣) غافر ٦٥ (٤٤) أول آل عمران (٤٥) الأنبياء ٣٠
Eighteenth: al-Wakil (the Trustee). Allah Most High said: 'And He is over all things a Trustee (Wakil)' (36). And creatures are described with it, so it is said: 'So-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so.'
Nineteenth: al-Mawla (the Patron). He said: 'That is because Allah is the Patron (Mawla) of those who believe, and that the disbelievers have no patron' (37). Then He said concerning us: 'And for each We have made heirs (mawla)' (38). And the Prophet said: 'For whomever I am his patron (mawla), 'Ali is his patron' (39).
Twentieth: al-Wali (the Ally). Allah Most High said: 'Your ally (wali) is only Allah and His Messenger and those who believe' (40). And the Prophet said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her guardian (wali), her marriage is invalid' (41). And He said: 'The believing men and believing women are allies (awliya') of one another' (42).
Twenty-first: al-Hayy (the Living). He said: 'He is the Living; there is no deity except Him' (43) - 'Alif Lam Mim. Allah, there is no deity except Him, the Living, the Self-Subsisting' (44). And He said: 'And We made from water every living thing' (45).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four divine names (al-asmā’ al-ḥusnā) in sequence, each with Quranic proof and often a note on how the same term applies to creation. Razi follows the same pattern for each entry: first he states the name, then he quotes one or more Quranic verses that establish it for Allah, and finally he shows that the term is also used for creatures, but in a limited or metaphorical sense.

For al-Wakīl (the Trustee), the verse from al-An‘ām 6:102 proves that Allah is over all things a Wakīl. Then Razi notes that creatures are described with this word, as in 'so-and-so is the wakil of so-and-so', but the meaning for Allah is unique and absolute.

For al-Mawlā (the Patron), he cites Muḥammad 47:11 stating that Allah is the Mawlā of believers, while disbelievers have no mawlā. He then mentions the verse from al-Nisā’ 4:33 which uses mawlā for human heirs, and the famous hadith 'man kuntu mawlāhu fa-‘Aliyyun mawlāhu', though he notes the hadith is āḥād (singular transmission). This shows the term applies to humans as well, but divine mawlā is superior.

For al-Walī (the Ally), Razi uses al-Mā’idah 5:55 to show Allah and the Messenger are the believers' walī. Then he cites a hadith about the necessity of a guardian for marriage, and al-Tawbah 9:71 where believers are awliyā’ of each other. Thus the term is shared, but the meaning differs: Allah's walī is unique and absolute.

For al-Ḥayy (the Living), he gives Āl ‘Imrān 3:2 and Ghāfir 40:65 asserting that Allah is the Living, and then al-Anbiyā’ 21:30 saying 'We made from water every living thing' for creatures, showing that created life is derived and limited.

This passage demonstrates Razi's method: he defends the divine names by scripture while acknowledging that the same Arabic words are used for contingent beings, but without any similarity in essence.

Key Terms
المولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if Allah is called Mawla, and humans are also called mawla, then the relationship is the same.
Correction: Allah's mawla-ship is incomparable; it denotes absolute authority and protection, whereas human mawla-ship is contractual and limited.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which group has no mawla according to the Quran?
A) Believers
B) Disbelievers
C) The Prophet
D) Heirs

Key Takeaway
Allah is the true Mawla of believers; the term also applies to human relationships but in a derivative sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever find yourself fearing human patrons more than Allah? What specific situation comes to mind, and how can you realign your trust today?
?
The hadith shows that allegiance to the Prophet entails allegiance to the believers. Is there any believer in your community whom you have difficulty accepting as a brother/sister? What step will you take to mend that relationship this week?
?
If Allah is your only true Mawla, what does that imply about your dependence on worldly authorities? Write one concrete area where you will consciously rely more on Allah and less on people starting right now.

Al-Wali: The Ally and Guardian
Razi presents al-Wali with Quranic verses and a hadith on guardianship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Wali is a divine name meaning the Ally and Guardian, supported by Quran and hadith, and that the term is also used for human guardians and allies.

Core Idea
Allah is the ultimate Wali; human guardianship is a shadow of His absolute wali-ship.

Razi presents the twentieth name, al-Wali, with a verse naming Allah and the Messenger as the believers' wali, then a hadith about marriage guardianship, and a verse about mutual alliance among believers. This shows the term's range from divine to human.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Wali. Identify the three pieces of evidence and their contexts.
The first verse is Surah al-Ma'idah (5:55).
2
Distinguish the Applications
Notice that Allah is called Wali absolutely, while humans are called wali in specific contexts: guardianship in marriage and mutual support among believers.
3
Reflect on the Shared Term
Consider why the same word is used and how the meanings differ in degree and kind.
This reinforces the concept of tanzih (transcendence).

Al-Hayy: The Living
Razi presents al-Hayy with Quranic proofs and the creaturely life from water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Hayy is a divine name meaning the Living, proven by multiple Quranic verses, and that creaturely life is derived from water and limited.

Core Idea
Allah is the Living who never dies; all created life is contingent and finite.

Razi concludes the sequence with the twenty-first name, al-Hayy, quoting verses that affirm Allah as the Living and Self-Subsisting. He also notes that creatures are described as living, as in the verse 'We made from water every living thing,' but this life is created and temporary.

1
Read the Passage

2
Compare Divine and Creaturely Life

3

Why is it important to distinguish between Allah's life and created life? This relates to the concept of qidam (eternity) and hadath (temporality).

?

The Divine Name Al-Wahid (the One)
Razi presents al-Wahid with Quranic proof and linguistic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Wahid and how it applies to Allah uniquely, even though the word 'one' is used for created things.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness is absolute and unlike any other 'one'.

Razi begins the twenty-second name, al-Wahid, by citing a Quranic verse that affirms Allah's exclusive oneness. He then notes that the same word 'one' is used for everyday objects like a garment or a person. This sets up the key theological principle: divine attributes are unique to Allah, even when the same Arabic term is applied to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
Read the Arabic line for al-Wahid. It states the name, then gives the verse from Qur'an 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' This is the direct proof.
Notice the concise style: 'al-Wāḥid' followed by 'qāla ta‘ālā'.
2
Observe the application to creation
Razi adds: 'And this description applies to most things, so it is said: one garment, one human.' Think about how the same word is used in everyday language.
This shows linguistic commonality but theological distinction.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on why Razi includes this. He is emphasizing that the attribute 'one' when applied to Allah is not like the 'oneness' of created things. Allah's oneness is absolute and exclusive.
This is a foundational point in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني والعشرون: الواحد . قال تعالى: «قل : إنما هو إله واحد (٤٦) ويقع هذا الوصف على أكثر الأشياء. فيقال: ثوب واحد ، وإنسان واحد .
الثالث والعشرون : التواب . قال تعالى: «إن الله كان توابا رحيما (٤٧) ويسمى الخلق به، فقال : « إن الله يحب التوابين (٤٨) ،
الرابع والعشرون : الغنى . قال تعالى : والله الغني (٤٩) - وقال : إنما السبيل على الذين يستأذنوك ، وهم أغنياء (٥٠) ، وقال في الأثر : خذها من أغنيائهم، وردها في فقراتهم ،
الخامس والعشرون : النور . قال الله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض (٥١) ، وقال : « نورهم يسعى بين أيديهم (٥٢) .
السادس والعشرون : الهادي . قال الله تعالى : « ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء (٥٣) ، وقال : « إنما أنت منذر ، ولكل قوم هاد (٥٤) ،
السابع والعشرون : المستمع . قال الله تعالى : وما ذهبا بآياتنا ، إنا معكم مستمعون (٥٥) ، وقال لموسى عليه السلام : « فاستمع لما يوحى (٥٦) ،
الثامن والعشرون : القديم . قال تعالى : حتى عاد كالعرجون القديم (٥٧) .
(٤٦) الأنعام ١٩ (٤٧) النساء ١٦ (٤٨) البقرة ٢٢٢ (٤٩) آخر محمد (٥٠) التوبة ٩٣ (٥١) النور ٣٥ (٥٢) التحريم ٨ (٥٣) القصص ٥٦ (٥٤) الرعد ٧ (٥٥) الشعراء ١٥ (٥٦) طه ١٣ (٥٧) ياسين ٣٩
Twenty-second: al-Wāḥid (the One). He said: 'Say: He is only one God' (46). And this description applies to most things, so it is said: 'one garment', 'one human'.
Twenty-third: al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance). He said: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47). And creation is named by it, so He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48).
Twenty-fourth: al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). He said: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) – and He said: 'The way is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50). And in the athar: 'Take it from their rich (aghniyā') and give it to their poor.'
Twenty-fifth: al-Nūr (the Light). Allah Most High said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51). And He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).
Twenty-sixth: al-Hādī (the Guide). Allah Most High said: 'But Allah guides whom He wills' (53). And He said: 'You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (54).
Twenty-seventh: al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing). Allah Most High said: 'And they did not go away with Our signs – indeed We are with you listening' (55). And He said to Moses, peace be upon him: 'So listen to what is revealed' (56).
Twenty-eighth: al-Qadīm (the Ancient). He said: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk' (57).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the enumeration of divine names, covering seven names from the 22nd to the 28th. Each entry follows the same pattern: the name is stated, then a Quranic verse is given to prove it for Allah, and finally a note shows how the same word is used for created things, indicating that the divine attribute is unique and not comparable to the created version.

For al-Wāḥid (the One), Razi quotes al-An‘ām 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' Then he notes that the description 'one' applies to most things, as in 'one garment' or 'one human', but the oneness of Allah is absolute and exclusive.

For al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance), he uses al-Nisā’ 4:16 and al-Baqarah 2:222. He adds that creation is also named by it, as in 'Allah loves those who repent' – humans are called 'tawwābīn' (those who repent), but Allah's acceptance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

For al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient), the verse from Muḥammad 47:38 asserts that Allah is al-Ghanī. Razi then cites al-Tawbah 9:93 about those who are 'aghniyā’' (rich), and an athar (tradition) about taking charity from the rich. Thus the term applies to creatures, but Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent.

For al-Nūr (the Light), he provides al-Nūr 24:35: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' and al-Taḥrīm 66:8 about the believers' light. Creatures have light, but it is created and limited, whereas Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and existence.

For al-Hādī (the Guide), he quotes al-Qaṣaṣ 28:56 and al-Ra‘d 13:7. Here, 'guide' (hādī) is used for Allah and for prophets, but the divine guidance is absolute and effective, while human guidance is mere conveyance.

For al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing), he uses al-Shu‘arā’ 26:15 for Allah and Ṭā Hā 20:13 for Moses. The hearing of Allah is without ears and encompasses all sounds, whereas human hearing is physical and limited.

For al-Qadīm (the Ancient), he cites Yā Sīn 36:39 about the old palm stalk. This name is particularly significant in kalām: 'ancient' (qadīm) is used for Allah alone in the sense of eternal, whereas created objects can be called 'old' in a temporal sense.

Thus, Razi demonstrates that while the Arabic language uses the same words for Allah and creation, the meanings are fundamentally different – a key principle of Sunni theological method.

Key Terms
الواحد
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that since 'one' is used for both Allah and creatures, Allah's oneness is similar to the oneness of a created object.
Correction: Razi shows the word 'one' applies to many things, but Allah's oneness is unique and absolute, not comparable to created oneness.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is cited as proof for the divine name al-Wahid?
A) Qur'an 6:19
B) Qur'an 4:16
C) Qur'an 47:38
D) Qur'an 24:35

Key Takeaway
The name al-Wahid affirms Allah's absolute oneness, distinct from any created 'one'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Ahad' in your prayers, do you truly feel in your heart that there is no other 'one' like Him? Or have you let worldly 'ones'—your career, your family, your desires—compete in your heart for the place that belongs only to the Unique One? Repent today and reaffirm His exclusive oneness in your actions.
?
Think of a time you felt Allah's oneness wasn't enough for you, when you turned to a created thing as your ultimate hope or refuge. What was that created 'one'? How does Razi's warning about the difference between created oneness and divine oneness pierce that idol in your heart? Now, what step will you take to uproot that reliance?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is al-Wahid—uniquely one in a way no creature can be—how would your daily anxieties change? For example, when you fear someone's opinion or trust in your own abilities, are you not putting another 'one' beside Him? This lesson calls you to a radical tawhid in your inner life. Will you respond today?

The Divine Name Al-Tawwab (the Acceptor of Repentance)
Razi presents al-Tawwab with Quranic proof and the name's application to creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-Tawwab and how Allah's acceptance of repentance is perfect, while created beings are called 'tawwabin' in a derivative sense.

Core Idea
Allah's acceptance of repentance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

The twenty-third name, al-Tawwab, is presented with a Quranic verse that describes Allah as 'ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful.' Razi then notes that humans are also called 'tawwabin' (those who repent), but the divine attribute is unique and free from need.

Guided Path
1
Read the entry for al-Tawwab
The passage states the name, then the verse from Qur'an 4:16: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful.' Read it carefully.
2
Notice the application to creation
Razi says: 'And creation is named by it, so He said: Indeed Allah loves those who repent.' Here, humans are called 'tawwabin'.
The word is used for both, but the meaning differs.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about how Allah's acceptance is perfect and self-sufficient, while human repentance is a created act that needs Allah's grace.
This reinforces tanzih (transcendence).

The Divine Name Al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient)
Razi presents al-Ghani with Quranic proof and linguistic examples from creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is al-Ghani, absolutely self-sufficient, while created wealth is a dependent attribute.

Core Idea
Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent of anything.

The twenty-fourth name, al-Ghani, is introduced with a direct Quranic statement: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient.' Razi then cites additional verses and a tradition that use the same word 'ghani' (rich) for people, showing that the human concept of wealth is a mere analogy for Allah's perfect independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says 'al-Ghani' followed by the verse from Surah Muhammad (47:38): 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient.'
2
Notice the additional evidence
Razi then quotes Qur'an 9:93 about 'those who are rich (aghniyā')' and an athar about taking from the rich. This shows the word is used for creatures.
The athar is a prophetic tradition or saying.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider how human wealth is temporary and dependent, while Allah's self-sufficiency is eternal and independent of all.
This highlights tanzih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your bank account, your job, or your health, do you see them as signs of Allah's Ghani or do you feel self-sufficient? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that only Allah is al-Ghani and you are in absolute need of Him at every second?
?
The athar says to take from the rich and give to the poor. If you are among the rich (even if only by a little), will you give something today that you consider 'yours' and not 'His'? What does your reluctance or willingness say about your belief in al-Ghani?
?
You know that Allah needs nothing from you—not your worship, not your money. Yet He commands you to worship and give. Does that make you love Him more, or does it expose a hidden expectation that you deserve something in return? Answer honestly before Him.

The Divine Name Al-Nur (the Light)
Razi presents al-Nur with two Quranic verses showing divine and created light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine name al-Nur as a metaphor for Allah's guidance and existence, distinct from physical light.

Core Idea
Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and self-manifestation.

The twenty-fifth name, al-Nur, is presented with the famous 'Light Verse' (Qur'an 24:35) and another verse about the believers' light. Razi uses this to show that while 'light' is used for both Allah and believers, the divine light is unique and incomparable.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage states al-Nur and then the verse: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth.' This is the primary proof.
2
Notice the second verse
Razi adds: 'Their light will run before them' from Qur'an 66:8, referring to the believers' light on the Day of Judgment.
This shows the word is applied to creatures.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about how Allah's light is not a physical light but a metaphor for guidance, existence, and knowledge. Created light is a physical phenomenon.
This is crucial for understanding tanzih.

الثاني والعشرون: الواحد . قال تعالى: «قل : إنما هو إله واحد (٤٦) ويقع هذا الوصف على أكثر الأشياء. فيقال: ثوب واحد ، وإنسان واحد .
الثالث والعشرون : التواب . قال تعالى: «إن الله كان توابا رحيما (٤٧) ويسمى الخلق به، فقال : « إن الله يحب التوابين (٤٨) ،
الرابع والعشرون : الغنى . قال تعالى : والله الغني (٤٩) - وقال : إنما السبيل على الذين يستأذنوك ، وهم أغنياء (٥٠) ، وقال في الأثر : خذها من أغنيائهم، وردها في فقراتهم ،
الخامس والعشرون : النور . قال الله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض (٥١) ، وقال : « نورهم يسعى بين أيديهم (٥٢) .
السادس والعشرون : الهادي . قال الله تعالى : « ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء (٥٣) ، وقال : « إنما أنت منذر ، ولكل قوم هاد (٥٤) ،
السابع والعشرون : المستمع . قال الله تعالى : وما ذهبا بآياتنا ، إنا معكم مستمعون (٥٥) ، وقال لموسى عليه السلام : « فاستمع لما يوحى (٥٦) ،
الثامن والعشرون : القديم . قال تعالى : حتى عاد كالعرجون القديم (٥٧) .
(٤٦) الأنعام ١٩ (٤٧) النساء ١٦ (٤٨) البقرة ٢٢٢ (٤٩) آخر محمد (٥٠) التوبة ٩٣ (٥١) النور ٣٥ (٥٢) التحريم ٨ (٥٣) القصص ٥٦ (٥٤) الرعد ٧ (٥٥) الشعراء ١٥ (٥٦) طه ١٣ (٥٧) ياسين ٣٩
Twenty-second: al-Wāḥid (the One). He said: 'Say: He is only one God' (46). And this description applies to most things, so it is said: 'one garment', 'one human'.
Twenty-third: al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance). He said: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47). And creation is named by it, so He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48).
Twenty-fourth: al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). He said: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) – and He said: 'The way is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50). And in the athar: 'Take it from their rich (aghniyā') and give it to their poor.'
Twenty-fifth: al-Nūr (the Light). Allah Most High said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51). And He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).
Twenty-sixth: al-Hādī (the Guide). Allah Most High said: 'But Allah guides whom He wills' (53). And He said: 'You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (54).
Twenty-seventh: al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing). Allah Most High said: 'And they did not go away with Our signs – indeed We are with you listening' (55). And He said to Moses, peace be upon him: 'So listen to what is revealed' (56).
Twenty-eighth: al-Qadīm (the Ancient). He said: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk' (57).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the enumeration of divine names, covering seven names from the 22nd to the 28th. Each entry follows the same pattern: the name is stated, then a Quranic verse is given to prove it for Allah, and finally a note shows how the same word is used for created things, indicating that the divine attribute is unique and not comparable to the created version.

For al-Wāḥid (the One), Razi quotes al-An‘ām 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' Then he notes that the description 'one' applies to most things, as in 'one garment' or 'one human', but the oneness of Allah is absolute and exclusive.

For al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance), he uses al-Nisā’ 4:16 and al-Baqarah 2:222. He adds that creation is also named by it, as in 'Allah loves those who repent' – humans are called 'tawwābīn' (those who repent), but Allah's acceptance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

For al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient), the verse from Muḥammad 47:38 asserts that Allah is al-Ghanī. Razi then cites al-Tawbah 9:93 about those who are 'aghniyā’' (rich), and an athar (tradition) about taking charity from the rich. Thus the term applies to creatures, but Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent.

For al-Nūr (the Light), he provides al-Nūr 24:35: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' and al-Taḥrīm 66:8 about the believers' light. Creatures have light, but it is created and limited, whereas Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and existence.

For al-Hādī (the Guide), he quotes al-Qaṣaṣ 28:56 and al-Ra‘d 13:7. Here, 'guide' (hādī) is used for Allah and for prophets, but the divine guidance is absolute and effective, while human guidance is mere conveyance.

For al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing), he uses al-Shu‘arā’ 26:15 for Allah and Ṭā Hā 20:13 for Moses. The hearing of Allah is without ears and encompasses all sounds, whereas human hearing is physical and limited.

For al-Qadīm (the Ancient), he cites Yā Sīn 36:39 about the old palm stalk. This name is particularly significant in kalām: 'ancient' (qadīm) is used for Allah alone in the sense of eternal, whereas created objects can be called 'old' in a temporal sense.

Thus, Razi demonstrates that while the Arabic language uses the same words for Allah and creation, the meanings are fundamentally different – a key principle of Sunni theological method.

Key Terms
النور
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Allah's light is a physical light similar to the sun or a lamp.
Correction: Razi shows that 'light' is used for believers as a created light, implying Allah's light is metaphorical and transcendent.

Checkpoint
Which verse speaks about the believers' light running before them?
A) Qur'an 24:35
B) Qur'an 66:8
C) Qur'an 47:38
D) Qur'an 6:19

Key Takeaway
Al-Nur means Allah is the source of guidance and existence, not a physical light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, where do you turn for guidance—are you actively seeking light from Allah (through Qur'an and sunnah) or are you chasing other sources that seem bright? What will you stop doing today to orient your heart towards Allah as the only true Light?
?
The believers' light on the Day of Judgment is described as 'running before them.' Does your current life reflect that you are walking in a path that will generate that light? If you were to examine your deeds today, would they be producing a light that runs ahead of you or a darkness that trails behind?
?
You have just read that Allah's light is uncreated and incomparable, yet you may still imagine Him in physical terms. Are you willing to let go of any mental image you have of Allah and accept His transcendence? What thought pattern about Allah do you need to repent from today?

The Divine Name Al-Hadi (the Guide)
Razi presents al-Hadi with two Quranic verses showing divine and prophetic guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is al-Hadi, the ultimate Guide, while human guides merely convey guidance.

Core Idea
Allah's guidance is absolute and effective; human guidance is only a means.

The twenty-sixth name, al-Hadi, is supported by two verses: one affirming that Allah guides whom He wills (Qur'an 28:56), and another stating that every nation has a guide (Qur'an 13:7). Razi thus distinguishes between divine guidance, which is creative and decisive, and human guidance, which is merely instructive.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says al-Hadi and then the verse from Qur'an 28:56: 'But Allah guides whom He wills.'
2
Notice the second verse
Razi adds 'And He said: You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (13:7). Here, a prophet is called a guide.
This shows the word is applied to creation.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider how Allah's guidance is effective and causes the guided person to be guided, while human guidance is just calling and teaching.
This emphasizes tawhid in guidance.

The Divine Name Al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing)
Razi presents al-Mustami‘ with two Quranic verses on divine and human listening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is al-Mustami‘, the All-Hearing, whose hearing is without ears and encompasses all, while human hearing is physical and limited.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing is perfect, without any bodily organ.

The twenty-seventh name, al-Mustami‘, is proven by a verse where Allah speaks to Moses and Aaron: 'Indeed We are with you listening.' Razi then quotes a second verse where Moses is commanded to listen. This shows that 'listening' is used for both Allah and humans, but the divine attribute is transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says al-Mustami‘ and then the verse from Qur'an 26:15: 'Indeed We are with you listening.' This is the direct proof.
2
Notice the second verse
Razi adds 'And He said to Moses: So listen to what is revealed' from Qur'an 20:13. Here, the command is for Moses to listen.
This shows the word is applied to a prophet.
3
Reflect on the difference
Think about how Allah's hearing is not with ears but is an attribute that encompasses all sounds without any instrument. Human hearing is physical and limited.
This is a key kalam point about divine attributes.

?

?
Allah's hearing is not like yours; it is without limitation. Does your awareness of His constant presence move you to seek His forgiveness for the words you have spoken carelessly? Commit to one specific change in your speech today as a direct response to this lesson.

The Divine Name Al-Qadim (the Ancient)
Razi presents al-Qadim with a Quranic verse showing a created thing described as 'old'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Qadim means Allah is eternal, without beginning, while created things are only relatively old.

Core Idea
Allah's 'ancientness' is eternal and uncreated, unlike temporal old age.

The twenty-eighth and final name in this passage, al-Qadim, is supported by a verse describing an old palm stalk. Razi thus shows that the word 'qadim' can refer to something created that has aged, but when applied to Allah, it signifies pre-eternity and self-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the name and its proof
The passage says al-Qadim and then the verse from Qur'an 36:39: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk.' The word 'old' here is qadim.
2
Understand the linguistic usage
Razi cites this verse to show that 'qadim' is used for a created object that has become old over time.
3
Reflect on the divine meaning
Consider that for Allah, 'Qadim' does not mean aged, but eternal without beginning. This is a key kalam term.
This name is particularly important in theological discussions about the pre-eternity of Allah.

الثاني والعشرون: الواحد . قال تعالى: «قل : إنما هو إله واحد (٤٦) ويقع هذا الوصف على أكثر الأشياء. فيقال: ثوب واحد ، وإنسان واحد .
الثالث والعشرون : التواب . قال تعالى: «إن الله كان توابا رحيما (٤٧) ويسمى الخلق به، فقال : « إن الله يحب التوابين (٤٨) ،
الرابع والعشرون : الغنى . قال تعالى : والله الغني (٤٩) - وقال : إنما السبيل على الذين يستأذنوك ، وهم أغنياء (٥٠) ، وقال في الأثر : خذها من أغنيائهم، وردها في فقراتهم ،
الخامس والعشرون : النور . قال الله تعالى : « الله نور السموات والأرض (٥١) ، وقال : « نورهم يسعى بين أيديهم (٥٢) .
السادس والعشرون : الهادي . قال الله تعالى : « ولكن الله يهدي من يشاء (٥٣) ، وقال : « إنما أنت منذر ، ولكل قوم هاد (٥٤) ،
السابع والعشرون : المستمع . قال الله تعالى : وما ذهبا بآياتنا ، إنا معكم مستمعون (٥٥) ، وقال لموسى عليه السلام : « فاستمع لما يوحى (٥٦) ،
الثامن والعشرون : القديم . قال تعالى : حتى عاد كالعرجون القديم (٥٧) .
(٤٦) الأنعام ١٩ (٤٧) النساء ١٦ (٤٨) البقرة ٢٢٢ (٤٩) آخر محمد (٥٠) التوبة ٩٣ (٥١) النور ٣٥ (٥٢) التحريم ٨ (٥٣) القصص ٥٦ (٥٤) الرعد ٧ (٥٥) الشعراء ١٥ (٥٦) طه ١٣ (٥٧) ياسين ٣٩
Twenty-second: al-Wāḥid (the One). He said: 'Say: He is only one God' (46). And this description applies to most things, so it is said: 'one garment', 'one human'.
Twenty-third: al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance). He said: 'Indeed Allah is ever Acceptor of Repentance, Merciful' (47). And creation is named by it, so He said: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent' (48).
Twenty-fourth: al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient). He said: 'And Allah is the Self-Sufficient' (49) – and He said: 'The way is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (50). And in the athar: 'Take it from their rich (aghniyā') and give it to their poor.'
Twenty-fifth: al-Nūr (the Light). Allah Most High said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (51). And He said: 'Their light will run before them' (52).
Twenty-sixth: al-Hādī (the Guide). Allah Most High said: 'But Allah guides whom He wills' (53). And He said: 'You are only a warner, and for every people there is a guide' (54).
Twenty-seventh: al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing). Allah Most High said: 'And they did not go away with Our signs – indeed We are with you listening' (55). And He said to Moses, peace be upon him: 'So listen to what is revealed' (56).
Twenty-eighth: al-Qadīm (the Ancient). He said: 'Until it returned like an old palm stalk' (57).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the enumeration of divine names, covering seven names from the 22nd to the 28th. Each entry follows the same pattern: the name is stated, then a Quranic verse is given to prove it for Allah, and finally a note shows how the same word is used for created things, indicating that the divine attribute is unique and not comparable to the created version.

For al-Wāḥid (the One), Razi quotes al-An‘ām 6:19: 'Say: He is only one God.' Then he notes that the description 'one' applies to most things, as in 'one garment' or 'one human', but the oneness of Allah is absolute and exclusive.

For al-Tawwāb (the Acceptor of Repentance), he uses al-Nisā’ 4:16 and al-Baqarah 2:222. He adds that creation is also named by it, as in 'Allah loves those who repent' – humans are called 'tawwābīn' (those who repent), but Allah's acceptance is perfect and originates from His mercy.

For al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient), the verse from Muḥammad 47:38 asserts that Allah is al-Ghanī. Razi then cites al-Tawbah 9:93 about those who are 'aghniyā’' (rich), and an athar (tradition) about taking charity from the rich. Thus the term applies to creatures, but Allah's self-sufficiency is absolute and independent.

For al-Nūr (the Light), he provides al-Nūr 24:35: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' and al-Taḥrīm 66:8 about the believers' light. Creatures have light, but it is created and limited, whereas Allah's light is a metaphor for His guidance and existence.

For al-Hādī (the Guide), he quotes al-Qaṣaṣ 28:56 and al-Ra‘d 13:7. Here, 'guide' (hādī) is used for Allah and for prophets, but the divine guidance is absolute and effective, while human guidance is mere conveyance.

For al-Mustami‘ (the All-Hearing), he uses al-Shu‘arā’ 26:15 for Allah and Ṭā Hā 20:13 for Moses. The hearing of Allah is without ears and encompasses all sounds, whereas human hearing is physical and limited.

For al-Qadīm (the Ancient), he cites Yā Sīn 36:39 about the old palm stalk. This name is particularly significant in kalām: 'ancient' (qadīm) is used for Allah alone in the sense of eternal, whereas created objects can be called 'old' in a temporal sense.

Thus, Razi demonstrates that while the Arabic language uses the same words for Allah and creation, the meanings are fundamentally different – a key principle of Sunni theological method.

Key Terms
القديم
الأسماء الحسنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Allah's 'oldness' means He has aged over time like created things.
Correction: Razi shows the word is used for a physically old object, so Allah's 'qadim' cannot mean the same; it means pre-eternal.

Checkpoint

A) A light
B) A palm stalk
C) A guide
D) A garment

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadim means Allah is eternal, without beginning, unlike temporal old age.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah as al-Qadim, eternal without beginning, do you truly feel the weight of His absolute supremacy, or do you subtly imagine Him as merely 'older' than everything? What in your daily life reveals whether you live with the reality of His pre-eternity?
?
If you deeply understood that Allah's 'oldness' is not like the aging of a palm stalk—that He never becomes older or weaker—would you fear growing old? How would your anxiety about time and decay change if you anchored your hope in the One who is not affected by time?
?
The verse describes a palm stalk returning to an old state. Are there parts of your faith—your prayer, your trust in Allah—that have become 'old' in a negative sense, routine and lifeless? How can you seek renewal from the Ever-Living who is not subject to decay?

Lists divine names 22-28 with Quranic evidence.

Seven divine names, each anchored in a Qur'anic verse, reveal distinct aspects of Allah's nature.

This passage continues the enumeration of the ninety-nine names, presenting names twenty-two through twenty-eight. Each name is introduced with a simple definition and supported by one or two verses from the Qur'an. As you read, notice how the author briefly notes linguistic usage for some names, such as 'al-Wahid' also applying to created objects, while primarily focusing on establishing the scriptural basis for each divine attribute.

Commentary

The footnotes at the end provide the Quranic chapter and verse references for each citation, confirming the source of each proof.

Affirming Divine Attributes Without Likeness
Introduction to the shared principles and the first Sunni approach to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first school affirms divine attributes like hand and istiwa' while strictly avoiding anthropomorphism and modality.

Core Idea
Affirmation without comparison: we say 'hand' but not 'like our hands'.

The passage first establishes the undisputed foundations of tawhid and tanzih. Then it presents a group of scholars, led by Ibn Khuzayma, who affirm the transmitted attributes exactly as reported, while emphatically denying any resemblance to creation. This approach balances the affirming verses with the negating verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the foundational principles
Read the first part where the author states the agreed-upon tawhid and tanzih. Notice that all Muslims agree on these.
2
Identify the first school's method
Then read how they affirm attributes like 'hand' and 'istiwa'' while explicitly denying any similarity or modality. They say: 'hand not like our hands'.
This is the core of the salaf method: ithbat bi-la tamthil.
3
Examine the balance of verses
Notice how they use both affirming verses (e.g., 'the hand of Allah is above their hands') and negating verses (e.g., 'there is none equal to Him') to maintain tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا : إن الله - عز وجل بين في القرآن الكريم : انه إله واحد، ولا إله غيره. وبين: أنه ليس كمثله شيء. والمسلمون لا يختلفون في الوحدانية ، وفي التنزيه الله - عز وجل – وإنما يقول بعضهم : هو واحد ، له يد ، ليست كأيدينا - ومنهم الشيخ ابن خزيمة - ورجل ، ليست كارجلنا، ويستوى على العرش ، بدون لا تمثيل لكيفية الاستواء. وهكذا يثبتون الصفات الخبرية ، الواردة في القرآن والحديث عن الله . وهذا الإثبات لظاهر الأخبار التي تثبتها . مثل : « يد الله فوق أيديهم ، وينفون التشبيه والتمثيل . وهذا النفى لظاهر الأخبار التي تنفيها . مثل : د ولم يكن له كفوا أحد، أى ليس له مثل ولا كف. ولا نظير . ويقول بعض المسلمين : هو واحد . ليس له جسم . لأن الجسم يتخيله العقل على أى شبه كان . وحيث نفى عن نفسه التشبيه . فإذن لا يكون جسما . ومأورد في الأخبار عن يده ورجله وعينه ، وسائر الأعضاء التي تدل على أن الله جسم : تؤول على معنى : القدرة وبجى الأمر ، وأنه يسمع ويرى ، وما شابه ذلك . ومن هؤلاء المسلمين الشيخ محمد بن عمر ، مؤلف أساس التقديس ، - في علم الكلام - وهؤلاء يقولون أيضا : إن الذي ورد في القرآن وفي الأحاديث من أن الله له صفات تشبه صفات البشر ، من المكر والخداع والاستحياء والغضب والرضا والمحبة والسكره، و ماشابه ذلك. ليس على الحقيقة . وإنما معناه : أن الله يتحدث عن للبشر ، كأنه واحد منهم ، ليقرب ذاته إلى عقوطم . وهو ليس مثلهم ، ولا مثل أى شىء د وقد أسندت هذه الأعمال إلى الله من باب المشاكلة . وهي تسمية الجزاء على الشيء ، باسم ذلك الشي.
Secondly, Allah - the Mighty and Majestic - clarified in the Noble Qur'an that He is one God, and there is no god besides Him. And He clarified that there is nothing like Him. Muslims do not differ regarding monotheism and regarding the transcendence (tanzih) of Allah - the Mighty and Majestic - rather some of them say: He is one, He has a hand, not like our hands - among them is the shaykh Ibn Khuzayma - and a leg, not like our legs, and He establishes Himself on the Throne (istiwa') without tashbih of the modality of istiwa'. Thus they affirm the transmitted attributes (al-sifat al-khabariyya) that are reported in the Qur'an and Hadith about Allah. And this affirmation is due to the apparent meaning of reports that affirm them, like 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', and they deny tashbih and imitation. And this denial is due to the apparent meaning of reports that deny them, like 'And there is none equal to Him', i.e., He has no equal, no match, no counterpart. And some Muslims say: He is one, He has no body, because the intellect imagines a body in any form whatsoever. Since He denied tashbih of Himself, therefore He is not a body. And what is reported in the traditions about His hand, His leg, His eye, and all the members that indicate that Allah is a body: they are interpreted (tu'awwal) to mean power, [؟] the command, and that He hears and sees, and the like. Among these Muslims is the shaykh Muhammad ibn 'Umar, author of Asas al-Taqdis, in kalam. And these also say: That which has been reported in the Qur'an and the Hadith that Allah has attributes resembling those of humans, like plotting, deceit, modesty, anger, pleasure, love, drunkenness, and the like, are not literal (haqiqa). Rather their meaning is that Allah speaks to humans as if He were one of them, to bring His essence closer to their intellects. And He is not like them, nor like anything. And these actions have been attributed to Allah by way of mushakala (figurative usage), which is naming the recompense for a thing by the name of that thing.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary contrasting two authoritative Sunni approaches to the divine attributes, especially the transmitted attributes (al-sifat al-khabariyya). The author first establishes the agreed-upon principles: Allah is one (tawhid) and transcendent (tanzih), as stated in the Qur'an. He notes that no Muslim disputes these. Then he presents the first approach, exemplified by Ibn Khuzayma and others: they affirm attributes like hand, leg, and istiwa' (establishing on the Throne) but reject any comparison (tashbih) or modality (takyif). They take the apparent meaning of affirming verses (e.g., 'the hand of Allah is above their hands') and also take the apparent meaning of negating verses (e.g., 'there is none equal to Him'), thus balancing affirmation and negation without figurative interpretation (ta'wil). The second approach, attributed to Imam al-Razi and his school, denies that Allah has a body because bodies are subject to the imagination and Allah has negated likeness. Therefore, any bodily terms in scripture are interpreted metaphorically: 'hand' means power, 'eye' means perception, etc. This group also interprets anthropomorphic actions like plotting, deceit, anger as literal meanings, saying they are figurative expressions (mushakala) used to bring the divine near to human understanding. Mushakala is defined as naming the recompense of an action by the name of the action itself. The passage thus presents two valid methodologies within Sunni orthodoxy: the salaf method of affirmation without modality or comparison, and the kalam method of metaphorical interpretation to safeguard transcendence.

Key Terms
التنزيه
التشبيه
الصفات الخبرية
الإثبات بلا تمثيل
الاستواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the first school described in the passage, what do Muslims generally differ on?
A) They differ on the unity of Allah (tawhid).
B) They differ on the transcendence of Allah (tanzih).
C) They differ on how to approach the transmitted attributes.
D) They differ on whether Allah has a hand.

Key Takeaway
The first school affirms divine attributes without comparison or modality, balancing the affirming and negating verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Interpreting Anthropomorphic Expressions (Ta'wil and Mushakala)
Explanation of the second Sunni approach that interprets bodily attributes metaphorically using ta'wil and mushakala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second school interprets bodily attributes metaphorically using ta'wil and how mushakala explains anthropomorphic actions attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah speaks to us 'as if' one of us, but He is beyond likeness.

The second group, represented by Imam al-Razi, prioritizes tanzih to the point that they interpret any bodily reference in scripture as metaphorical. They argue that since Allah has no body, terms like hand must mean power, and actions like 'deceit' are figurative, using a device called mushakala, where the recompense takes the name of the action.

Guided Path
1
Read the second school's starting point
Read from 'ويقول بعض المسلمين' and note their key premise: Allah has no body, because He negates likeness and the intellect imagines a body in forms.
2
See how ta'wil is applied
Then they interpret hand, leg, eyes as power, command, and perception. This is ta'wil: explaining the apparent meaning in a way that fits tanzih.
They do not deny the attributes but reinterpret them.
3
Understand mushakala
Finally, they explain anthropomorphic actions like 'deceit' as mushakala: naming the recompense after the action. For example, Allah's 'mocking' the disbelievers means He recompenses them in a way like mockery, but He does not actually mock.
This safeguards tanzih while accepting the scriptural terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا : إن الله - عز وجل بين في القرآن الكريم : انه إله واحد، ولا إله غيره. وبين: أنه ليس كمثله شيء. والمسلمون لا يختلفون في الوحدانية ، وفي التنزيه الله - عز وجل – وإنما يقول بعضهم : هو واحد ، له يد ، ليست كأيدينا - ومنهم الشيخ ابن خزيمة - ورجل ، ليست كارجلنا، ويستوى على العرش ، بدون لا تمثيل لكيفية الاستواء. وهكذا يثبتون الصفات الخبرية ، الواردة في القرآن والحديث عن الله . وهذا الإثبات لظاهر الأخبار التي تثبتها . مثل : « يد الله فوق أيديهم ، وينفون التشبيه والتمثيل . وهذا النفى لظاهر الأخبار التي تنفيها . مثل : د ولم يكن له كفوا أحد، أى ليس له مثل ولا كف. ولا نظير . ويقول بعض المسلمين : هو واحد . ليس له جسم . لأن الجسم يتخيله العقل على أى شبه كان . وحيث نفى عن نفسه التشبيه . فإذن لا يكون جسما . ومأورد في الأخبار عن يده ورجله وعينه ، وسائر الأعضاء التي تدل على أن الله جسم : تؤول على معنى : القدرة وبجى الأمر ، وأنه يسمع ويرى ، وما شابه ذلك . ومن هؤلاء المسلمين الشيخ محمد بن عمر ، مؤلف أساس التقديس ، - في علم الكلام - وهؤلاء يقولون أيضا : إن الذي ورد في القرآن وفي الأحاديث من أن الله له صفات تشبه صفات البشر ، من المكر والخداع والاستحياء والغضب والرضا والمحبة والسكره، و ماشابه ذلك. ليس على الحقيقة . وإنما معناه : أن الله يتحدث عن للبشر ، كأنه واحد منهم ، ليقرب ذاته إلى عقوطم . وهو ليس مثلهم ، ولا مثل أى شىء د وقد أسندت هذه الأعمال إلى الله من باب المشاكلة . وهي تسمية الجزاء على الشيء ، باسم ذلك الشي.
Secondly, Allah - the Mighty and Majestic - clarified in the Noble Qur'an that He is one God, and there is no god besides Him. And He clarified that there is nothing like Him. Muslims do not differ regarding monotheism and regarding the transcendence (tanzih) of Allah - the Mighty and Majestic - rather some of them say: He is one, He has a hand, not like our hands - among them is the shaykh Ibn Khuzayma - and a leg, not like our legs, and He establishes Himself on the Throne (istiwa') without tashbih of the modality of istiwa'. Thus they affirm the transmitted attributes (al-sifat al-khabariyya) that are reported in the Qur'an and Hadith about Allah. And this affirmation is due to the apparent meaning of reports that affirm them, like 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', and they deny tashbih and imitation. And this denial is due to the apparent meaning of reports that deny them, like 'And there is none equal to Him', i.e., He has no equal, no match, no counterpart. And some Muslims say: He is one, He has no body, because the intellect imagines a body in any form whatsoever. Since He denied tashbih of Himself, therefore He is not a body. And what is reported in the traditions about His hand, His leg, His eye, and all the members that indicate that Allah is a body: they are interpreted (tu'awwal) to mean power, [؟] the command, and that He hears and sees, and the like. Among these Muslims is the shaykh Muhammad ibn 'Umar, author of Asas al-Taqdis, in kalam. And these also say: That which has been reported in the Qur'an and the Hadith that Allah has attributes resembling those of humans, like plotting, deceit, modesty, anger, pleasure, love, drunkenness, and the like, are not literal (haqiqa). Rather their meaning is that Allah speaks to humans as if He were one of them, to bring His essence closer to their intellects. And He is not like them, nor like anything. And these actions have been attributed to Allah by way of mushakala (figurative usage), which is naming the recompense for a thing by the name of that thing.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary contrasting two authoritative Sunni approaches to the divine attributes, especially the transmitted attributes (al-sifat al-khabariyya). The author first establishes the agreed-upon principles: Allah is one (tawhid) and transcendent (tanzih), as stated in the Qur'an. He notes that no Muslim disputes these. Then he presents the first approach, exemplified by Ibn Khuzayma and others: they affirm attributes like hand, leg, and istiwa' (establishing on the Throne) but reject any comparison (tashbih) or modality (takyif). They take the apparent meaning of affirming verses (e.g., 'the hand of Allah is above their hands') and also take the apparent meaning of negating verses (e.g., 'there is none equal to Him'), thus balancing affirmation and negation without figurative interpretation (ta'wil). The second approach, attributed to Imam al-Razi and his school, denies that Allah has a body because bodies are subject to the imagination and Allah has negated likeness. Therefore, any bodily terms in scripture are interpreted metaphorically: 'hand' means power, 'eye' means perception, etc. This group also interprets anthropomorphic actions like plotting, deceit, anger as literal meanings, saying they are figurative expressions (mushakala) used to bring the divine near to human understanding. Mushakala is defined as naming the recompense of an action by the name of the action itself. The passage thus presents two valid methodologies within Sunni orthodoxy: the salaf method of affirmation without modality or comparison, and the kalam method of metaphorical interpretation to safeguard transcendence.

Key Terms
التأويل
المشاكلة
الجسم
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the second school completely denies the attributes or claims that scripture is false.
Correction: The second school affirms the attributes but interprets them metaphorically to uphold tanzih, using ta'wil and mushakala.

Checkpoint
According to the second school, why are bodily attributes like 'hand' interpreted metaphorically?
A) Because the Quranic text is unclear.
B) Because Allah has no body, so the terms must refer to something else.
C) Because the Prophet explained them that way.
D) Because the scholars prefer rationalism over revelation.

Key Takeaway
The second school uses ta'wil and mushakala to interpret anthropomorphic expressions, ensuring that Allah's transcendence remains inviolate while scripture is upheld.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah in a human-like way, even subconsciously? What step will you take tonight to remind yourself that He is absolutely unlike anything?
?
If Allah speaks to us 'as if' one of us, what does that imply about His care for our understanding? How should that shape the way you approach difficult verses in your daily recitation?

Imam al-Shafi'i on Quranic Language and Divine Attributes
Editorial quote of Imam al-Shafi'i explaining Quranic anthropomorphic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Shafi'i interprets anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran by distinguishing between human language and divine reality.

Core Idea
Quranic anthropomorphic expressions are for human understanding, not literal divine attributes.

This lesson presents a key hermeneutical principle from Imam al-Shafi'i regarding divine attributes. He explains that the Quran speaks about Allah in human language to convey meanings, not to imply resemblance. He then applies this to the verse 'And He is easier upon Him' (30:27), showing how the apparent meaning must be understood in light of human estimation, not divine capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to al-Shafi'i's overall statement about Quranic language before tackling the specific verse.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the hermeneutical principle
Al-Shafi'i opens by saying the Quran speaks about Allah and His attributes 'bi-lisan bani Adam' (in the tongue of the children of Adam). This is the key principle: divine speech uses human conceptual categories for communication.
This principle guards against literalism.
3
See the principle applied to the verse
Read how al-Shafi'i explains 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi'. He argues that 'ahwan' (easier) refers to human estimation, not divine capacity. The example of creating from non-existence vs. restoring something illustrates this.
The word 'al-'ibra' (estimation) is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قال الإمام الشافعي - رضي الله عنه في هذه الصفات ، كلاماً معناه : إن القرآن تحدث عن الله وصفاته بلسان بني آدم . قال في قوله تعالى : ( وهو أهون عليه ، : « معناه : في العبرة عندكم . لأنه لما قال للعدم كن، فيخرج تاماً ، كاملا بعينيه وأذنيه وبصره وأنفه وسمعه ومفاصله . فهذا فى العبرة أشد من أن يقول لشيء قد كان وفى : عد إلى ما كنت . فالمراد من الآية : وهو أهون عليه بحسب عرفكم ، لا أن شيئاً يكون أهون على الله تعالى من شيء آخر
And Imam al-Shafi'i - may Allah be pleased with him - said regarding these attributes, a statement whose meaning is: That the Qur'an spoke about Allah and His attributes in the tongue of the children of Adam. He said regarding the verse 'And He is easier upon Him' (Quran 30:27): 'Its meaning is: in estimation among you. Because when He says to non-existence 'be', it emerges complete, perfect with its eyes, ears, sight, nose, hearing, and joints. This in estimation is more severe than saying to something that has been and gone: return to what you were. So the intended meaning of the verse is: 'And He is easier' according to your custom, not that anything is easier upon Allah Almighty than another thing.'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Imam al-Shafi'i, as transmitted by the editor, regarding the interpretation of divine attributes. Al-Shafi'i first establishes a hermeneutical principle: the Qur'an speaks about Allah and His attributes using the language of humans (bi-lisan bani Adam). This means that when the Qur'an attributes human-like actions or qualities to Allah, it does so in a way that humans can understand, not that Allah literally resembles them. He then applies this principle to the verse 'And He is easier upon Him' (wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi) from Surah Ar-Rum (30:27), which some might take to imply that some things are 'easier' for Allah than others, suggesting limitation. Al-Shafi'i explains that the word 'ahwan' (easier) here refers to human estimation (fi al-'ibra 'indakum). He provides a striking example: when Allah commands non-existence to 'be' (kun) and a fully formed human being emerges with all its limbs, that is, in human estimation, more remarkable than restoring something that previously existed. So the verse means: 'it is easier in your estimation, O people' – it is a way of communicating a point about divine power using human conceptual categories. The actual meaning is not that one act is literally easier for Allah than another, because Allah is omnipotent and nothing is difficult for Him. Thus al-Shafi'i reinforces the principle that scriptural expressions must be understood in light of how language conveys meaning to humans, avoiding any suggestion of divine limitation or anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
لسان بني آدم
العبرة
أهون
العدم
القول كن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'wa huwa ahwanu 'alayhi' means that resurrecting is literally easier for Allah than initial creation.
Correction: Al-Shafi'i clarifies that 'ahwan' refers to human estimation (al-'ibra), not an actual difference in divine power. In human view, creating from nothing is more astonishing than restoring, but for Allah all acts are equally effortless.

C) It indicates that some things are difficult for Allah.
D) It is a command to make things easier for believers.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Shafi'i teaches that Quranic expressions about divine attributes must be interpreted according to human linguistic usage, not as literal descriptions of Allah's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Shafi'i says the Qur'an speaks in human language so you can understand. But are you willing to submit to that interpretation, or do you insist on a 'clearer' answer that suits your human logic? What does that insistence reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom over your own?
?
The phrase 'bi-lisan bani Adam' means Allah speaks in your language. But do you ever unconsciously expect Allah to adapt to your expectations of how He should be described? Al-Shafi'i's principle demands that you accept that divine speech is accommodated to human understanding, not that Allah resembles what you understand. ?
Al-Shafi'i's example of creation from nothing being 'more remarkable' in human estimation is meant to show that Allah's power is incomparable. But do you live as if that is true? Consider a worry you face today: does your trust in Allah's ability to create from nothing affect how you view that problem?
